Divine Terms: Mercy, Guardianship, and More
Imam Razi lists additional divine terms and gives Ash'ari's definitions, all as types of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of additional divine terms such as mercy, guardianship, hatred, and choice according to Islamic theologians, especially Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
Ash'ari systematically defines all divine actions as expressions of will, reducing apparent attributes to variations of irada.

After defining love, Imam Razi continues with a series of other terms: mercy, guardianship, hatred, punishment, and choice. Each is defined by Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari as a specific type of will (irada). This lesson clarifies these definitions and notes the Karamiyya's exception.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of definitions for each term
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the pattern: each term is defined as a specific kind of will.
The terms are presented in rapid succession.
2
Notice the Ash'ari reduction to will
For each term, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari defines it as irada (will) with a specific object: mercy is will to bestow favor, guardianship is will to honor, etc.
This shows a consistent theological method.
3
Understand the consistency
All divine actions and attributes are reducible to God's will. Hatred, enmity, and punishment are also wills—to humiliate or torment.
This avoids positing multiple eternal attributes.
4
Note the Karamiyya's distinction
The passage ends by noting that the Karamiyya distinguish between mashi'a (will) and irada (will), unlike the mainstream view.
This is a minority position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : العزم عبارة عن إرادة جازمة ) بعد التردد فيه ، والمحبة عبارة عن الإرادة لكنها من الله تعالى فى حق العبد إرادة الثواب ، ومن العبد فى حق الله تعالى إرادة الطاعة والرضا ، قيل إنه الإرادة وقيل إنه ترك الاعتراض (۲) (۲) أقول : التردد المذكور يحصل من الدواعي المختلفة المنبعثة عن الآراء العقلية وعن الشهوات والنفرات المتخالفة ، فإن لم يوجد ترجيح لطرف حصل التحير وإن وجد حصل العزم، والمحبة تقع باشتراك الاسم على إرادة هو مبدأ فعل وهو الذى نسبه إلى إرادة الثواب أو الطاعة وعلى تصور كمال من لذة أو منفعة أو مشاكلة كحبة العاشق لمعشوقه والمنعم عليه المنعمه والوالد لولده و أما محبة الله سبحانه وتعالى عند العارفين فهو لتصور الكمال المطلق فيه والرضاء . قال أبو الحسن الأشعرى : إنه إرادة إكرام المؤمنين ومثوبتهم على التأييد وهذا من الله تعالى ، وأما من العبد فهو ترك الاعتراض، والرحمة قيل هى النعمة وقال أبو الحسن هي إرادة الإنعام، والولاية إرادة الإكرام والتوفيق ، والبغض والعداوة إرادة الإهانة ، والطرد والتعذيب والسخط إرادة التعذيب، والاختيار عند أبى الحسن هو الإرادة واختار له أى فعل به خيراً ، والمشيئة هى الإرادة ، والكرامية يفرقون بينهما .
Issue: Resolution ('azm) is an expression of a decisive will (irada jazima) after hesitation in it. Love (mahabbah) is an expression of will, but from God Almighty with respect to the servant it is will of reward, and from the servant with respect to God Almighty it is will of obedience and good pleasure. It is said that it is will, and it is said that it is leaving objection (tark al-i'tirad). (2) I say: The aforementioned hesitation occurs from different motives (dawa'i) emanating from intellectual opinions, desires (shahawat) and divergent repulsions. If there is no preponderance for a side, bewilderment occurs; if there is, resolution occurs. Love (mahabbah) occurs by homonymy (ishtirak al-ism) on will that is a principle of action, which is what he attributed to will of reward or obedience, and on conceiving a perfection of pleasure, benefit, or similarity (mushakala) like the love of the lover for his beloved, the benefactor for the one he has favored, and the parent for his child. As for love of God Almighty according to the gnostics ('arifin), it is for conceiving absolute perfection in Him and good pleasure (rida). Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: It is the will to honor the believers and reward them forever, and this is from God. As for from the servant, it is leaving objection (tark al-i'tirad). Mercy (rahma) is said to be blessing (ni'ma), and Abu al-Hasan said it is the will to bestow favor (irada al-in'am). Guardianship (wilaya) is the will to honor and grant success. Hatred (bughd) and enmity ('adawa) are the will to humiliate. Expulsion (tard), punishment (ta'dhib) and displeasure (sakhat) are the will to torment. Choice (ikhtiyar) according to Abu al-Hasan is will, and 'He chose for him' means He did a good thing for him. The will (mashi'a) is the will (irada), and the Karamiyya distinguish between them.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage defines several key terms. Resolution ('azm) is a decisive will after hesitation, which arises from conflicting motives. Love (mahabbah) is ambiguously applied: it can mean a will that is the principle of action (as in God's will of reward for believers and the servant's will of obedience), or it can mean a conceptual appreciation of perfection (as in human love based on pleasure, benefit, or similarity). For gnostics, love of God is appreciation of His absolute perfection. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari provides systematic definitions: love from God is will to honor and reward believers; from the servant it is non-objection. Similarly, mercy is will to bestow favor; guardianship is will to honor; hatred and enmity are will to humiliate; punishment-related terms are will to torment; choice is will; will (mashi'a) is synonymous with irada, though the Karamiyya differentiate them.

Key Terms
الرحمة (al-rahma)
الولاية (al-wilaya)
البغض (al-bughd)
العداوة (al-'adawa)
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyar)
المشيئة (al-mashi'a)
الكَرّامية (al-Karamiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mercy, hatred, etc. are separate eternal attributes of God.
Correction: Ash'ari defines them all as specific types of will (irada), not as independent attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, what is God's mercy (rahma)?
A) The act of bestowing blessings
B) The attribute of compassion
C) The will to bestow favor (irada al-in'am)
D) The absence of punishment

Key Takeaway
For Ash'ari, all divine terms like mercy, guardianship, and hatred are specific expressions of God's will, not independent attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience hardship or calamity, do you recognize it as part of God's will to humiliate or test? How does that reshape your response to pain? Commit to saying 'I accept Your will' the next time you face difficulty.
?
If every divine term like mercy and hatred is simply a variation of God's will, then nothing happens outside His will. Is there any area of your life where you still believe your successes or failures are beyond God's will? Will you trace every outcome back to His will today?
?
The Ash'ari definition of 'choice' (ikhtiyar) is simply will. Reflect on a major life decision you made. Can you honestly say you chose it only because God willed it for you? Will you now surrender your future choices to His will, trusting that He chooses what is good for you?

The Proof for Internal Speech as a Distinct Attribute
Razi presents the Ash'ari proof that command, prohibition, and information are intelligible meanings distinct from letters and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that command, prohibition, and information are distinct intelligible meanings, not reducible to will, letters, or other attributes, thereby establishing internal speech as a divine attribute.

Core Idea
Command, prohibition, and information are distinct meanings that cannot be reduced to will or letters.

This passage from Imam al-Razi presents one of the key Ash'ari proofs for the attribute of internal speech (kalam al-nafs). The Ash'aris argue that the commands, prohibitions, and information in scripture are not mere external letters or sounds, nor are they identical to God's will. Instead, they are intelligible meanings that exist as a distinct eternal attribute. This lesson unpacks that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
The passage starts by stating that only the Ash'aris affirm internal speech. Then it lists three types: command, prohibition, information.
2
Understand the rejection of letters
The passage argues that these meanings are not the imagination of letters because letters vary but meanings are constant.
3
Distinguish command from will
The passage gives the crucial argument: God may command what He does not will, so command is not will.
4
See the conclusion
Since it is not knowledge, power, or life, it must be a distinct entity: internal speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها كلام النفس ولم يقل به أحد إلا أصحابنا قالوا الأمر والنهي والخير أمور معقولة يعبر عن كل واحد منها في كل لغة بلفظة أخرى فهى معان متغايرة وليس عبارة عن تخيل الحروف ، لأن تخيلها تابع لها ومختلف باختلافها وهذه الماهيات لا تختلف البتة ، وليس الأمر عبارة عن الإرادة لأن الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد ويريد ما لا يأمر به ، وظاهر أنه ليس عبارة عن العلم والقدرة والحياة فلا بد من نوع واحد . أقول : قالوا كلام النفس هو الفكر الذي يدور في الخلد وتدل عليه العبارات تارة وما يصطلح عليه من الإشارات أخرى ، والدليل على إثباته أن الفاعل إذا أمر عبده بأمر وجد في نفسه اقتضاء الطاعة منه وجدانا ضروريا ثم إنه يدا على ما يجده ببعض العبارات أو بضروب من الإشارات أو برقوم من هكذا قيل : وقيل أبو هاشم أثبت كلاما في النفس سماه بالخواطر وزعم أن ذا الخاطر يسمعها ويدركها ، وقال أبو الحسن إن لفظ الكلام يقع على كلام النفس وعلى الكلام المؤلف من الحروف بالاشتراك ، وقال قوم على الأول بالحقيقة ، وعلى الثانى بالمجاز ، وقال قوم بالعكس من ذلك
And among them is internal speech (kalam al-nafs), and no one held it except our companions (the Ash'aris). They said: Command, prohibition, and information are intelligible things, each expressed in every language by a different word; they are distinct meanings, and are not an expression of imagining letters, because their imagination follows them and differs with their difference, and these essences do not differ at all. Command is not an expression of will because God Almighty may command what He does not will and will what He does not command. It is apparent that it is not an expression of knowledge, power, or life, so there must be one type. I say: They said internal speech is the thought that circulates in the mind, and expressions sometimes indicate it, and agreed-upon signs at other times. The proof for establishing it is that when an agent commands his servant to do something, he finds in himself a demand (iqdida) for obedience from him, necessarily by intuition; then he indicates what he finds by some expressions or kinds of signs or symbols. Thus it is said. And it was said that Abu Hashim established a speech in the soul which he called 'khawatir' (thoughts), and he claimed that the one having the thought hears and perceives them. Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) said that the word 'speech' applies to internal speech and to speech composed of letters by homonymy. Some said to the first truly and to the second metaphorically, and some said the opposite.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage explains the Ash'ari doctrine of internal speech (kalam nafs). It begins by noting that only the Ash'aris affirm it. Command, prohibition, and information are intelligible meanings (ma'qulat) that are expressed differently in each language; they are not the imagining of letters because letters are dependent and vary, while these meanings are unchanging. They argue that command is not identical to will, because God may command what He does not will (e.g., commanding something He knows will not happen) and will what He does not command. Similarly, it is not knowledge, power, or life, so it must be a distinct entity: internal speech. The commentary then defines internal speech as thought (fikr) in the mind, indicated by words and gestures. The proof: when a master commands a servant, he finds in himself an inner demand (iptida') for obedience instinctively, then expresses it. Abu Hashim called these 'khawatir' (inner thoughts). Al-Ash'ari held that the term 'speech' applies to both internal and external speech by homonymy. There is disagreement on which is literal and which metaphorical.

Key Terms
كلام النفس
أمر
نهي
خبر
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's command is identical to His will or to the physical words revealed.
Correction: The Ash'aris argue that command is a distinct eternal meaning, not will nor letters, because God may command what He does not will.

Checkpoint
Why do the Ash'aris argue that command (amr) is not the same as will (irada)?
A) Because will is eternal, while command is temporal
B) Because God may command what He does not will and will what He does not command
C) Because command is only in letters
D) Because will is a different category

Key Takeaway
God's command, prohibition, and information are distinct intelligible meanings, not reducible to will or letters, establishing the attribute of internal speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear God's command in the Quran, do you treat it as a genuine directive that God really means, or do you reduce it to something else to avoid its demand on your life? What would change if you believed this command is an eternal, distinct attribute of God?
?
The passage says God may command what He does not will. Does this distinction make you reconsider how you understand God's commands in your own life—are there commands you have ignored thinking He did not really will them for you?
?
If God's prohibition is a distinct intelligible meaning in His essence, how should that change your view of sin? Is it merely a social construct or a personal preference, or a real, eternal reality that you are accountable to?

Defining Internal Speech: Thought in the Mind and Scholarly Disagreement
Razi defines internal speech and presents the proof from inner demand, then discusses scholarly views on the term 'speech'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris define internal speech as thought in the mind, the proof from inner demand, and the disagreement between Abu Hashim and al-Ash'ari on the nature of this speech.

Core Idea
Internal speech is the thought in the mind, and scholars differ on whether the term 'speech' applies literally to it.

Having established that internal speech is a distinct attribute, this passage defines it more concretely as thought (fikr) that circulates in the mind, indicated by expressions and gestures. A second proof is given: when a master commands, he finds an inner demand for obedience. The passage then records the views of Abu Hashim, who called these thoughts 'khawatir,' and al-Ash'ari, who held that the word speech applies to both internal and external speech by homonymy, with disagreement on which is literal.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of internal speech
The passage defines it as thought in the mind, expressed by words and signs.
2
Understand the proof from inner demand
The proof: when commanding, one finds an instinctive demand for obedience.
3
Note Abu Hashim's view
He called it 'khawatir' (thoughts) and claimed they can be heard.
4
Learn al-Ash'ari's position
The term 'speech' applies to both by homonymy, with some saying internal is literal, others saying external is literal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها كلام النفس ولم يقل به أحد إلا أصحابنا قالوا الأمر والنهي والخير أمور معقولة يعبر عن كل واحد منها في كل لغة بلفظة أخرى فهى معان متغايرة وليس عبارة عن تخيل الحروف ، لأن تخيلها تابع لها ومختلف باختلافها وهذه الماهيات لا تختلف البتة ، وليس الأمر عبارة عن الإرادة لأن الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد ويريد ما لا يأمر به ، وظاهر أنه ليس عبارة عن العلم والقدرة والحياة فلا بد من نوع واحد . أقول : قالوا كلام النفس هو الفكر الذي يدور في الخلد وتدل عليه العبارات تارة وما يصطلح عليه من الإشارات أخرى ، والدليل على إثباته أن الفاعل إذا أمر عبده بأمر وجد في نفسه اقتضاء الطاعة منه وجدانا ضروريا ثم إنه يدا على ما يجده ببعض العبارات أو بضروب من الإشارات أو برقوم من هكذا قيل : وقيل أبو هاشم أثبت كلاما في النفس سماه بالخواطر وزعم أن ذا الخاطر يسمعها ويدركها ، وقال أبو الحسن إن لفظ الكلام يقع على كلام النفس وعلى الكلام المؤلف من الحروف بالاشتراك ، وقال قوم على الأول بالحقيقة ، وعلى الثانى بالمجاز ، وقال قوم بالعكس من ذلك
And among them is internal speech (kalam al-nafs), and no one held it except our companions (the Ash'aris). They said: Command, prohibition, and information are intelligible things, each expressed in every language by a different word; they are distinct meanings, and are not an expression of imagining letters, because their imagination follows them and differs with their difference, and these essences do not differ at all. Command is not an expression of will because God Almighty may command what He does not will and will what He does not command. It is apparent that it is not an expression of knowledge, power, or life, so there must be one type. I say: They said internal speech is the thought that circulates in the mind, and expressions sometimes indicate it, and agreed-upon signs at other times. The proof for establishing it is that when an agent commands his servant to do something, he finds in himself a demand (iqdida) for obedience from him, necessarily by intuition; then he indicates what he finds by some expressions or kinds of signs or symbols. Thus it is said. And it was said that Abu Hashim established a speech in the soul which he called 'khawatir' (thoughts), and he claimed that the one having the thought hears and perceives them. Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) said that the word 'speech' applies to internal speech and to speech composed of letters by homonymy. Some said to the first truly and to the second metaphorically, and some said the opposite.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage explains the Ash'ari doctrine of internal speech (kalam nafs). It begins by noting that only the Ash'aris affirm it. Command, prohibition, and information are intelligible meanings (ma'qulat) that are expressed differently in each language; they are not the imagining of letters because letters are dependent and vary, while these meanings are unchanging. They argue that command is not identical to will, because God may command what He does not will (e.g., commanding something He knows will not happen) and will what He does not command. Similarly, it is not knowledge, power, or life, so it must be a distinct entity: internal speech. The commentary then defines internal speech as thought (fikr) in the mind, indicated by words and gestures. The proof: when a master commands a servant, he finds in himself an inner demand (iptida') for obedience instinctively, then expresses it. Abu Hashim called these 'khawatir' (inner thoughts). Al-Ash'ari held that the term 'speech' applies to both internal and external speech by homonymy. There is disagreement on which is literal and which metaphorical.

الخواطر
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think internal speech is just a metaphor for external speech and not a real attribute.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari held that the term applies by homonymy, and some scholars consider internal speech the literal meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did al-Ash'ari say about the term 'speech'?
A) It applies only to external speech
B) It applies to internal speech by homonymy
C) It applies to internal speech literally
D) It applies only to internal speech

Key Takeaway
Internal speech is defined as thought in the mind, and the term 'speech' is used homonymously for both internal and external speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You speak to yourself in your mind countless times a day. Do you realize that this internal speech is real and known to Allah? What would change if you treated every internal thought as a form of speech for which you will be accountable?
?
Consider a recent internal conversation you had with yourself—perhaps justifying a sin or rationalizing a mistake. Is that internal speech truthful? How can you align your inner dialogue with the truth you claim to believe?
?
The scholars disagreed on whether the word 'speech' applies literally to internal thoughts. But for you, what is the weight of your own internal words? If they were made audible for all to hear, would you be ashamed? Commit today to purify one recurring internal thought.

Sight is Distinct from Knowledge
Al-Razi distinguishes sight from knowledge using experiential evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that sight is not identical to knowledge, using the example of seeing and then looking away.

Core Idea
Sight and knowledge are different: you can know something without seeing it.

Before refuting false theories of vision, al-Razi first establishes that sight is a distinct perception from knowledge. He uses a simple experiential example to show that even when we stop seeing an object, we still have knowledge of it. This sets the stage for discussing the true nature of sight, especially in reference to Allah's attribute of seeing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sight versus knowledge
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how al-Razi uses a concrete experience to make his point.
The OCR has 'غب' which likely is 'غير' (other than).
2
Identify the key example
Al-Razi says: we see a thing, then we turn away. In both states, knowledge is present, but the experience of seeing is only in the first state. Therefore, sight is not knowledge.
3
Understand the theological stake
Al-Razi mentions that the philosophers and others claim sight is merely an impression in the eye. Theologians must refute this to uphold that Allah is 'seeing' (basir) without a physical organ.
This links to the next lesson on refuting theories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الإدراكات وهى غب العلم لا انا نبصر الشيء ثم نغيب عنه فيفيق تفرقة في الحالتين مع حصول العلم فيهما فالابصار غير العلم لكن الفلاسفة والكمي وأبو الحسين زعموا أنه عائد إلى تأثر الحدقة بصيرة المرئى والمتكلمون محتاجون إلى القدح في هذا الاحتمال ليمكنهم بيان أنه تعالى بصير مسألة اختلفوا فى الإبصار منهم من قال إنه بخروج الشعاع عن العين وهو باطل وإلا لوجب تشوش الأبصار عند هبوب الرياح ولا متنع أن يرى نصف السماء لامتناع أن يخرج من حدقتنا ما يتصل بكل هذه الأشياء أو يؤثر في جميع الأجسام ومنهم من قال بالانطباع وهو باطل وإلا لما أدركنا العظيم لامتناع انطباع العظيم في حلقتنا
And among them are perceptions. They are other than knowledge. Indeed, we see a thing then we turn away from it, and one notices a distinction between the two states with the presence of knowledge in both; so sight is other than knowledge. But the philosophers, al-Kammi, and Abu al-Husayn claimed that it reduces to the impression of the image in the pupil, and the theologians need to refute this possibility to be able to prove that He (Allah) is seeing. Issue: They differed concerning vision. Some said it is by emission of a ray from the eye, and it is false; otherwise, sight would be disturbed when winds blow, and it would be impossible to see half the sky because it is impossible for a ray to exit from our pupil that connects to all these things or affects all bodies. And some said it is by impression, and it is false; otherwise, we would not perceive large things because of the impossibility of the large thing being impressed in our small pupil.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage addresses the nature of perceptions, specifically clarifying that sight (al-ibsar) is not identical to knowledge. Al-Razi argues by example: when we see something and then look away, we still have knowledge of it, but the sight state differs, proving sight is separate. He then presents the dispute on the mechanism of vision. Two theories are refuted: (1) the ray emission theory (khuruj al-shu'a') — if a ray exited the eye, it would be disturbed by wind and could not cover half the sky; (2) the impression theory (al-inṭiba') — if the image were impressed in the eye, large objects could not fit. The theologians must refute these theories to uphold the divine attribute of sight (Allah is seeing) without relying on physical mechanisms.

Key Terms
إدراكات
إبصار
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think sight is identical to knowledge or a form of knowledge.
Correction: Al-Razi shows through experience that sight and knowledge are distinct: you can have knowledge without sight.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi prove that sight is not knowledge?
A) By quoting scripture.
B) By noting that knowledge remains after sight ceases.
C) By arguing that sight is a physical process.
D) By stating that knowledge is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Sight and knowledge are distinct; knowledge can exist without sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you turn away from contemplating Allah's creation, do you still carry the same vivid awareness of Him, or does your knowledge become distant and theoretical? How can you make your knowledge of Allah as constant as the knowledge you have of a seen object after you look away?
?
If sight and knowledge are distinct, then knowing about Allah is not the same as perceiving Him directly. Are you content with mere knowledge, or do you truly long for the vision of Him in the Hereafter? What changes would that longing cause in your life today?
?
Al-Razi shows that sensory evidence can be misleading—what you see is not identical to what you know. Where in your faith are you relying too heavily on what you can see or feel, neglecting the firm knowledge that remains when those experiences fade?

Refuting Theories of Vision
Al-Razi refutes two false theories of vision: ray emission and impression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's arguments against the ray emission and impression theories of vision.

Core Idea
Vision does not occur by a ray exiting the eye nor by an image impressing in it.

Having established that sight is distinct from knowledge, al-Razi now addresses the two main theories proposed to explain the mechanism of vision. He refutes both the 'ray emission' theory (held by some ancient philosophers) and the 'impression' theory. His arguments are based on physical impossibilities that anyone can observe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two theories
Read the Arabic and translation covering the two false theories. Notice the structure: first the ray theory and its refutation, then the impression theory and its refutation.
The word 'شعاع' means ray, 'انطباع' means impression.
2
Analyze the refutation of the ray theory
Al-Razi gives two reasons: 1) winds would disturb the ray, but we see despite wind. 2) A ray from a small pupil cannot cover half the sky.
3
Analyze the refutation of the impression theory
If an image were impressed in the eye, large objects like a mountain could not fit. But we do see large objects, so the theory is false.
4
Connect to the theological goal
By refuting physical mechanisms, al-Razi clears the way for asserting that Allah sees without a physical organ or process.
This supports the attribute of sight (basir) for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الإدراكات وهى غب العلم لا انا نبصر الشيء ثم نغيب عنه فيفيق تفرقة في الحالتين مع حصول العلم فيهما فالابصار غير العلم لكن الفلاسفة والكمي وأبو الحسين زعموا أنه عائد إلى تأثر الحدقة بصيرة المرئى والمتكلمون محتاجون إلى القدح في هذا الاحتمال ليمكنهم بيان أنه تعالى بصير مسألة اختلفوا فى الإبصار منهم من قال إنه بخروج الشعاع عن العين وهو باطل وإلا لوجب تشوش الأبصار عند هبوب الرياح ولا متنع أن يرى نصف السماء لامتناع أن يخرج من حدقتنا ما يتصل بكل هذه الأشياء أو يؤثر في جميع الأجسام ومنهم من قال بالانطباع وهو باطل وإلا لما أدركنا العظيم لامتناع انطباع العظيم في حلقتنا
And among them are perceptions. They are other than knowledge. Indeed, we see a thing then we turn away from it, and one notices a distinction between the two states with the presence of knowledge in both; so sight is other than knowledge. But the philosophers, al-Kammi, and Abu al-Husayn claimed that it reduces to the impression of the image in the pupil, and the theologians need to refute this possibility to be able to prove that He (Allah) is seeing. Issue: They differed concerning vision. Some said it is by emission of a ray from the eye, and it is false; otherwise, sight would be disturbed when winds blow, and it would be impossible to see half the sky because it is impossible for a ray to exit from our pupil that connects to all these things or affects all bodies. And some said it is by impression, and it is false; otherwise, we would not perceive large things because of the impossibility of the large thing being impressed in our small pupil.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage addresses the nature of perceptions, specifically clarifying that sight (al-ibsar) is not identical to knowledge. Al-Razi argues by example: when we see something and then look away, we still have knowledge of it, but the sight state differs, proving sight is separate. He then presents the dispute on the mechanism of vision. Two theories are refuted: (1) the ray emission theory (khuruj al-shu'a') — if a ray exited the eye, it would be disturbed by wind and could not cover half the sky; (2) the impression theory (al-inṭiba') — if the image were impressed in the eye, large objects could not fit. The theologians must refute these theories to uphold the divine attribute of sight (Allah is seeing) without relying on physical mechanisms.

Key Terms
شعاع
انطباع
حدقة
إبصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think vision involves either a ray from the eye or an image entering the eye.
Correction: Al-Razi shows both are physically impossible: ray emission is disturbed by wind and cannot cover large areas; impression cannot accommodate large objects.

Checkpoint
What is al-Razi's objection to the impression theory of vision?
A) It contradicts scripture.
B) Large objects cannot be impressed in the small pupil.
C) The impression would be upside down.
D) It requires a medium.

Key Takeaway
Both theories of vision (ray emission and impression) are false because they lead to absurd physical consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you gaze at the sky or a mountain, do you ever stop and realize that your seeing them defies the simple physical explanations of ray or impression? What does this tell you about the limitations of human reasoning when it comes to divine actions? How will you let this truth change your trust in your own assumptions?
?
Al-Razi's arguments show that if vision were by a ray, it would be disturbed by wind. Have you ever assumed that something you experience must have a direct physical cause, only to later realize that Allah's creation is far more direct and non-material? What practical step can you take today to rely more on Allah and less on 'mechanisms'?
?
There is a common tendency to think that we 'understand' how we see. After reading this passage, how does your certainty that Allah's sight is unlike anything you can imagine affect your awe and humility before Him? What specific habit can you begin to cultivate that reflects this recognition?

Divine Vision: Unconditioned Sight
Razi states the Ash'ari position on divine vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that divine vision is not subject to the conditions and instruments necessary for human sight.

Core Idea
God's vision is unlike ours—unconditioned, without instrument, and unobstructed.

Imam al-Razi begins by distinguishing the Ash'ari view from that of the Mu'tazila and philosophers regarding divine vision. For the Ash'aris, God's vision is a distinct attribute not reducible to knowledge and is free from the limitations of created sight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the opening phrase that sets the Ash'ari position.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify that divine vision is not identical to knowledge, not caused by any connection, not by an instrument, and not subject to veiling.
3
Contrast with other views
Understand that this directly opposes the Mu'tazilite and philosopher view that God's vision is simply knowledge.
This contrast will be detailed in the next lesson.
4
Reflect on implications
Think about why this matters for tawhid—God's perfection means His attributes are beyond creaturely limitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: الله [?] ئلون بأن إبصار الله تعالى للموجودات غير علمه بالمبصرات لا يقولون بوجوب الإبصار عند الرابطة المذكورة لامتناع أن يكون إبصاره بآلة وأن يحجبه شيء عن شيء. وأما المعتزلة والفلاسفة فيقولون: إبصاره تعالى هو علمه بالمبصرات ويوجبون إبصار الخلق عند عشر شرائط بعد سلامة الآلة: وهي كون المبصر كثيفا غير مفرط الصغر، ومحاذيا للآلة أو في حكم المحاذاة زمانا، والمتوسط بينهما شفاف، ووقوع الضوء على المبصر، وكون الضوء غير مفرط، وعدم الغرب المفرط والبعد المفرط، وأن يتعهد الإبصار ذو آلة الإبصار، وأن لا يقاربه ما يوجب الغلط، ويدعون في وجوب الإبصار العلم الضروري. وأما تعليل رؤية الكبير صغيرا برؤية بعض أجزائه دون بعض فليس بشيء، فإن ذلك يقوله من لا يعرف السبب فيه. ومعارضة الشك في ذلك بالعاديات هو أن يقال: من المحتمل أن الشمس لا تطلع غدا، وأن الجبال الغائبة عنا صارت جواهر والبحار دما، وأمثال ذلك، مع أنا نجزم بعدمها بسبب إجراء العادة. كذلك ههنا: من المحتمل أن لا يبصر مع اجتماع الشرائط، لأنا نقطع بالإبصار ولا يلتفت إلى ذلك الاحتمال، لأن العادة جارية بالإبصار.
I say: God [unclear word] say that God's vision of existents is not His knowledge of visible things; they do not affirm the necessity of vision at the mentioned connection, because it is impossible that His vision be by an instrument and that anything veil Him from anything. As for the Mu'tazila and the philosophers, they say: His vision is His knowledge of visible things, and they affirm the necessity of the vision of created beings at ten conditions after the soundness of the instrument: that the visible object be dense, not excessively small, and facing the instrument or in the ruling of facing in time, and the medium between them be transparent, and the light falling on the visible object, and the light not be excessive, and the absence of excessive haze and excessive distance, and that the possessor of the visual instrument intend vision, and that nothing causing error be near it; and they claim for the necessity of vision necessary knowledge. As for the explanation of seeing the large as small by seeing some of its parts without others, it is nothing, because that is said by one who does not know the reason for it. And the rebuttal of doubt about that by customs is that it could be said: it is possible that the sun will not rise tomorrow, and that the hidden mountains have become jewels and the seas blood, and similar things, even though we are certain of their non-existence due to the running of custom. Similarly here: it is possible that one does not see despite the gathering of conditions, but we are certain of vision and do not pay attention to that possibility, because custom runs with vision.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a clarification about divine vision and the conditions for human vision. The author distinguishes the Ash'ari position (that God's vision is not identical to His knowledge and is not subject to conditions or instruments) from the Mu'tazilite and philosopher position (that God's vision is His knowledge, and human vision requires ten specific conditions after the instrument is sound). The ten conditions are enumerated: the visible object must be dense, not too small, facing the eye or effectively facing with time, the medium transparent, light present and not excessive, no excessive haze or distance, the observer must intend to see, and nothing causing error should be near. They claim that when these conditions are met, vision is necessary by immediate knowledge. The author then critiques the explanation that seeing a large object as small is due to seeing only parts, dismissing it as ignorance of the true cause. He defends the counter-argument based on custom (al-'adah): just as we are certain the sun will rise tomorrow because of custom, we are certain vision occurs with the conditions because God's custom decrees it, not logical necessity.

Key Terms
إبصار الله (ibsar Allah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's vision is like human vision, requiring eyes and light.
Correction: God's vision is unconditioned, without instrument, and cannot be obstructed.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT true of divine vision according to the Ash'ari position?
A) It is distinct from knowledge.
B) It requires an instrument.
C) Nothing can veil it.
D) It is not subject to conditions.

Key Takeaway
Divine vision is free from the conditions and instruments of human sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God sees you without any veil—every thought, every hidden action—are you living as if He sees? What one thing today will you change because nothing is hidden from His sight?
?
Your perception of God is limited by your human senses. How does knowing that God's vision is unconditioned and unlike yours humble you? Will you seek to see His signs more consciously today?
?
God's vision does not depend on instruments, but you depend on your eyes. Are you using your eyes to look at what pleases Him or what distracts you from Him? What will you look away from starting now?

The Ten Conditions of Human Vision
Razi enumerates the ten conditions for human vision according to Mu'tazila and philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten conditions that the Mu'tazila and philosophers hold necessary for human vision.

Core Idea
Human vision depends on ten specific physical and circumstantial conditions.

After stating the Ash'ari view, al-Razi presents the opposing view of the Mu'tazila and philosophers. They assert that when the instrument of sight is sound and ten conditions are met, vision is necessarily produced. These conditions cover the object, the medium, the instrument, and the observer.

Guided Path
1
Locate the enumeration
Find the part where the ten conditions are listed in the Arabic passage.
2
Understand each condition
Read the translation and note each condition: object's density, size, facing, medium transparency, light, light intensity, haze, distance, intention, and absence of error.
These are physical and intentional conditions.
3
Note the claim of necessary knowledge
The Mu'tazila and philosophers claim that when these conditions are met, vision is known necessarily (al-'ilm al-daruri).
This claim is challenged by the Ash'ari custom argument later.
4
Reflect on the philosophical basis
Think about why they require these conditions—seeing as a natural process governed by physical laws.

Refuting a Flawed Explanation and the Custom Argument
Razi critiques a false explanation and defends certainty of vision via custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's critique of a flawed explanation for seeing large as small, and his argument from custom to justify certainty of vision.

Core Idea
Custom, not logical necessity, grounds our certainty in vision under conditions.

In the final part of the passage, al-Razi addresses two issues. First, he dismisses the idea that seeing a large object as small is due to seeing only part of it, calling it ignorant. Then he presents a rebuttal: even if vision is not logically necessary, we are still certain of it because God's customary practice (adah) ensures it, just as we are certain of the sun rising.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique
Examine the sentence where al-Razi dismisses the explanation of seeing a large object as small.
Pay attention to his strong dismissal: 'it is nothing'.
2
Understand the custom argument
Read the rebuttal of doubt using the example of the sun rising and other regularities.
This is an Ash'ari argument against necessary causation.
3
Compare the two certainties
Notice how the same reasoning applies to vision: possible non-occurrence but certainty due to custom.
4
Reflect on the role of custom
Think about how custom (adah) functions in Ash'ari theology as a basis for empirical certainty.
This connects to the broader topic of causality.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

Where is your trust placed: in your actions or in Allah's custom of reward? Examine your heart honestly: do you rely on the custom or on the One who established it?

The Theologians' Stance and the Philosophers' Argument
Razi states the theologians' consensus and presents the philosophers' argument involving blackness and whiteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' consensus that an accident cannot subsist in another accident and the philosophers' argument that appears to show it can.

Core Idea
Accidents must ultimately subsist in a substance, not in another accident.

This passage opens with the theologians' unanimous rejection of an accident subsisting in another accident, based on the need to end at a substance. The philosophers counter with an example: blackness and whiteness share the accident of color-ness, implying accident-in-accident subsistence. This lesson unpacks that foundational dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the theologians' position
Start with the first sentence: the theologians agree that an accident cannot subsist in another accident. Understand their basis: everything must ultimately rest in a substance.
Note the term 'قيام العرض بالعرض' (subsistence of accident in accident).
2
Examine the philosophers' example
The philosophers argue that blackness and whiteness share color-ness (lowniyya). Since sharing and differing are distinct, color-ness must be an accident subsisting in blackness and whiteness.
This uses the principle: 'ما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز' (that by which they share is other than that by which they differ).
3
Follow the inherence regress argument
The philosophers also argue that the inherence relation itself, if not identical to the accident or substrate, would be another accident, leading to an infinite chain.
This is the second sub-argument: 'فكون العرض حالا في المحل ليس نفس العرض...'
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this infinite regress is problematic for the theologians. The argument seems to show that accidents can inhere in accidents, contradicting the initial claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : أتفق المتكلمون على امتناع قيام العرض بالمرض خلافاً للفلاسفة) ومعولنا أنه لابد من الانتهاء بالآخرة إلى الجوهر وحينئذ يكون الكل في حيز الجوهر فبالأصل فالكل قائم به ، احتجوا بأن السواد يشارك البياض في اللونية ويخالفه في السوادية والبياضية ، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز ، فالمونية صفة مغايرة للسواد قائمة به ، وهما موجودان لانه لا واسطة بين الوجود والعدم فاللونية عرض قائم بالسوادية ، وأيضاً فكون العرض حالا في المحل ليس نفس العرض ونفس المحل ،، لصحة أن يعقلا مع الذهول عن ذلك الحلول ، وليس أيضاً أمراً عدمياً لأنه نقيض اللاحاول فهو صفة قائمة بذلك العرض، ثم الكلام فيه كما في الأول ، فهنا أعراض لا نهاية لها يقوم كل واحد منها بالآخر ، والجواب عنها بمقدمات تقدم تقريرها (۲) أقول : وجوب الانتهاء إلى ما يقوم بالجوهر لا يدل على امتناع قيام البعض بالبعض وقيام البعض الأخير بالجوهر، والقائل بإمكان قيام العرض بالعرض مقر بأن الانتهاء لا يمكن أن يكون إلا إلى الجوهر ، وإنما الخلاف في التوسط هل يمكن أو لا، وهو لم يتعرض لذلك ، وما أورده في احتجاج القائلين بذلك ليس بصحيح ، لأنه أقام الصفات فيها مقام الأعراض والصفة مالا تعقل إلا مع غيره ، والعرض مالا يوجد إلا في غيره ، وقيام بعض الصفات ببعض الأجسام لا يوجب قيام بعض الأعراض ببعض، أما اللونية الجنس للسوادية ← ( ١٥ - المحصل )
Issue: The theologians agreed on the impossibility of the subsistence of an accident in an accident, contrary to the philosophers. Our basis is that ultimately one must end at the substance, and then everything is in the domain of the substance, so essentially everything subsists in it. They argued that blackness shares with whiteness in color-ness and differs in blackness-ness and whiteness-ness, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ; so color-ness is a quality different from blackness subsisting in it, and both exist because there is no intermediate between existence and non-existence; so color-ness is an accident subsisting in blackness-ness. Also, the fact that an accident is in a substrate is not itself the accident nor the substrate, since they can be conceived while disregarding that inherence; nor is it a privative thing because it is the opposite of non-inherence, so it is a quality subsisting in that accident. Then the same discussion applies as before. Here there are accidents without end, each subsisting in another. The answer to them is based on premises that have been established earlier. (I say: The necessity of ending at what subsists in the substance does not prove the impossibility of some subsisting in others and the last one subsisting in the substance. The one who posits the possibility of an accident subsisting in an accident admits that the end can only be the substance; the disagreement is only about mediation – whether it is possible or not – and he did not address that. What he presented in the argument of those who accept that is not correct, because he substituted attributes for accidents, and an attribute is only conceivable with another, while an accident only exists in another. The subsistence of some attributes in some bodies does not necessitate the subsistence of some accidents in others. As for color-ness, it is the genus of blackness-ness... [→ p.15 of al-Muhassal].)
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the theological stance against the philosophical view that an accident can inhere in another accident. The theologians argue that the chain must end at a substance. The philosophers present an example: blackness and whiteness share in color-ness (lowniyya), which is a quality distinct from each, so color-ness is an accident subsisting in blackness. Also, the inherence relation (hulul) itself is neither the accident nor the substrate, and it is not a privation, so it must be an accident subsisting in the accident. This leads to an infinite regress of accidents. Razi's note responds that the argument is flawed: the philosophers confuse attributes (sifat) with accidents (a'rad). An attribute is only understood with another, whereas an accident only exists in another. The subsistence of attributes in bodies does not imply subsistence of accidents in accidents. He also points out that the dispute is about whether mediation (tawassut) is possible, not about the final endpoint. The note ends with a reference to another part of the book.

Key Terms
قيام العرض بالعرض
الجوهر
السواد
البياض
اللونية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the theologians deny any inherence of accidents, but they affirm accidents inhere in substances.
Correction: The theologians only deny accident-in-accident subsistence, not accident-in-substance.

Checkpoint
Why do the theologians argue that accidents cannot subsist in accidents?
A) Because accidents are too simple
B) Because it would lead to an infinite regress without ending at a substance
C) Because the Quran explicitly forbids it
D) Because accidents are not real entities

Key Takeaway
The dispute centers on whether accidents can serve as substrates for other accidents, with the theologians requiring a substance as the ultimate foundation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers' argument shows that without an ultimate substance, every attribute rests on another until infinity. Which belief in your life have you left ungrounded, trusting that it will hold without an ultimate foundation? Will you trace it back to its root today?
?
If every quality must inhere in something, then even your most abstract thoughts are anchored in a reality. What does that say about the One who created both substance and accident? Are you living as if Allah is the only self-subsisting reality?
?
The theologians insisted on ending the regress with a substance. But you allow countless distractions and desires to pile up, each relying on the other for meaning. What would it mean for you to stop the regress and anchor your soul in the Eternal? Name one concrete step you will take this week to cut the chain and direct your heart solely to Allah.

Razi's Response: Distinguishing Attributes from Accidents
Razi refutes the philosophers' argument and explains the confusion between attributes and accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique of the philosophers' argument and his distinction between attributes and accidents.

Core Idea
Attributes are not accidents; their subsistence does not imply accident-in-accident.

Razi responds to the philosophers' argument by clarifying that they have confused attributes (sifat) with accidents (a'rad). While attributes are only conceivable with another, accidents exist in another. This distinction resolves the apparent challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's initial observation
Start with 'وجوب الانتهاء إلى ما يقوم بالجوهر...'. Razi notes that the necessity to end at a substance does not forbid intermediate accidents.
The real dispute is about mediation (tawassut), not the final endpoint.
2
Understand the distinction between attribute and accident
Razi says the philosophers substituted attributes for accidents. An attribute (sifa) is only conceivable with another, whereas an accident (arad) only exists in another.
This is a key terminological distinction: 'الصفة مالا تعقل إلا مع غيره، والعرض مالا يوجد إلا في غيره'.
3
Apply the distinction to the example
Color-ness (lowniyya) is a genus (jins), not an accident. Its subsistence in blackness does not imply accident-in-accident, because it is an attribute, not an accident.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Razi's critique shows that the philosophers' argument is based on a category mistake. Once we properly distinguish attributes from accidents, the infinite regress is avoided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Impossibility of Motion and Its Implications
Razi examines the necessity of motion's impossibility in a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument about whether the impossibility of motion is essential to a body and how it applies to the world's eternity.

Core Idea
Impossibility cannot be caused if it is essential, and the same logic refutes the opponent.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses an objection about motion being impossible for a body. He examines whether this impossibility is essential or accidental, and shows that the opponent's own argument leads to a contradiction regarding the world's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two possibilities: the impossibility of motion is either essential to the essence or accidental.
3
Follow the logical move
If essential, it remains forever; if accidental, it implies the world could be eternal, refuting the opponent's claim.
This is a classic dialectical turn.
4
Understand the initial response
Razi first says impossibility is non-existence and uncaused, but then grants causation for argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله امتناع الحركة إما أن يكون لازما للماهية أو لا يكون قلنا الامتناع عدم فلا يعلل، سلمنا كونه معللا لكنه لازم ووارد عليكم أيضا فإن العالم يمتنع أن يكون أزليا فهذا الامتناع إن كان لازما للماهية وجب أن يبقى ممتنعا أبدا وإن لم يكن لازما كان هذا اعترافا بحراز كون العالم أزليا وذاك يبطل قولكم . أما الوجه الثاني فنقول : لا نسلم كون السكون وصفا ثبوتيا سلمناه لكن لا نسلم افتقاره إلى المؤثر لان علة الحاجة عندكم الحدوث فلا يمكنكم بيان افتقار هذا السكون إلى المؤثر إلا إذا بينتم حدوثه وأنتم فرعتم حدوثه على هذه المقدمة فيصير ديرا . سلمناه لكن لا نسلم أن القديم لا ينعدم، فإن الله تعالى تاير من الأزل إلى الأبد على إيجاد العالم ، فبعد أن أوجه ما بقيت تلك القادرية لأن إيجاد الموجود محال ، فقد عدم ذلك التعلق القديم . وأيضا لا يقال إنه سبحانه وتعالى قادر على إيجاده بواسطة أن يعدمه ثم يعيده مرة أخرى ، لانا نقول كلامنا في إثبات ذلك النحلق المخصوص أعنى تعلق قدرته بإيجاد العالم ابتداء وهذا الذي ذكرتموه تعلق آخر ، ينتقض بأن الله تعالى كان عالما في الأزل بأن العالم معدوم ، فإذا أوجده فقد زال ذلك العلم القديم . والجواب عن الأول : أنه لا بداية لا مكان حدوث العالم ، لكن لا يلزم منه صحة كون العالم أزايا ، كما أنا إذا أخذنا هذا الحادث بشرط كونه مسبوقا لعدم سبقا زمانيا فإنه لا أول لصحة وجيده مع هذا الشرط وإلا فسينتهي في فرض المقدم إلى حيث لو وجد قبله بلحظة صار أزليا وذلك محال، ثم على فرض أنه لابداية لهذه الصحة لم يلزم صحة كونه أزليا لما أن الأزلية وسبق العدم بالزمان لا يجتمعان فكذلك . وعن الثاني : أن تقدم عدم العالم على وجوده وتقدم وجود الله تعالى على وجود العالم عندنا كنقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض عندكم ، وكما أن ذلك التقدم ليس بالزمان وإلا لزم التسلسل فكذا هذا . وعن الثالث : أنا إذا فرضنا جوهرين متميزين متماسين فنعنى بالسكون بقاءهما على هذا الوجه وبالحركة أن لا تبقى تلك المماسة بل يصير مماما لشيء آخر وعلى هذا التفسير لا حاجة إلى بيان ماهية المكان .
He said: The impossibility of motion is either necessary for the essence or not. We said: Impossibility is non-existence, so it is not caused. We grant that it is caused, but it is necessary, and the same objection applies to you: the world cannot be eternal. If this impossibility is necessary for the essence, then it must remain impossible forever; if it is not necessary, then this is an acknowledgment of the fact that the world is eternal, and that refutes your statement. As for the second aspect, we say: We do not grant that rest (sukun) is a positive attribute. We grant it, but we do not grant that it needs an effecter, because according to you the cause of need is temporality (huduth). So you cannot demonstrate that this rest needs an effecter unless you demonstrate its temporality, and you have based its temporality on this premise, resulting in circularity. We grant it, but we do not grant that the eternal does not cease to exist, for God Almighty is [intending?] from eternity to eternity to bring the world into existence. After He brings it into existence, that power remains [valid?] because bringing into existence what already exists is impossible, so that eternal relation has ceased. Also, it cannot be said that He is able to bring it into existence by means of annihilating it and then recreating it, for we say: our discussion is about proving that specific relation, namely the relation of His power to bring the world into existence initially, and what you mentioned is another relation. It is refuted by the fact that God knew in eternity that the world was non-existent; when He brings it into existence, that eternal knowledge vanishes. The answer to the first: There is no beginning to the possibility of the world's occurrence in time, but it does not imply the world is truly eternal. For if we take this temporal event with the condition that it is preceded by non-existence not in a temporal sense, then there is no beginning to the validity of its existence with this condition; otherwise it would end in a premise such that if it existed a moment earlier it would be eternal, which is impossible. Then, assuming that this possibility has no beginning, it does not imply that it is eternal, because eternality and temporal precedence of non-existence are incompatible. As for the second: The precedence of the world's non-existence over its existence and the precedence of God's existence over the world's existence is, according to us, like the precedence of some parts of time over others according to you. And just as that precedence is not temporal (otherwise it would lead to infinite regress), so too here. As for the third: If we suppose two distinct, contiguous substances, we mean by rest the preservation of their [relative] state, and by motion the non-preservation of that contiguity such that [one] becomes contiguous with something else. On this interpretation, there is no need to define the nature of place.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage addresses multiple objections. The first objection (قوله امتناع الحركة) concerns the impossibility of motion: if it is essential to the body, then it must always be impossible; if not essential, then it can change. The author responds that impossibility is a privation (adam) and thus not caused, but even if caused, the same logic applies to the opponent's claim that the world cannot be eternal. The second objection (أما الوجه الثاني) deals with rest as a positive attribute requiring a cause. The author argues that even if rest is positive, the need for a cause arises from temporality, so the opponent cannot prove rest's temporality without circularity. The third objection concerns the non-annihilation of the eternal; the author denies that the eternal cannot cease, using God's power as an example: the relation of power to bring the world into existence ceases after creation. He also addresses an objection about re-creation and the loss of eternal knowledge. Then he provides three answers: (1) the possibility of the world's temporal occurrence has no beginning but that does not imply eternal actuality; (2) the precedence of non-existence over existence is non-temporal; (3) motion and rest can be defined without reference to place, using the concept of contiguity between substances.

Key Terms
الامتناع
الماهية
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Impossibility is always caused by something.
Correction: Impossibility as a pure non-existence (adam) does not require a cause; only positive attributes do.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, if the impossibility of motion is necessary for the essence, what follows?
A) It would be impossible forever
B) It could become possible
C) It needs a cause
D) It proves the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of something can be essential and unchanging, or accidental and changeable, and this distinction must be applied consistently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about what is impossible for God (e.g., that He cannot have a partner or a son), is that impossibility essential to your understanding of His nature, or could it be dismissed as accidental? How does the distinction in this lesson affect your certainty about your faith's most foundational claims?
?
You have just seen an argument that impossibility can be turned back on the one who uses it. Are you ready to apply the same logical scrutiny to your own positions as you do to others'? Is there a belief you hold about the world that, if subjected to this same test, might force you to admit an inconsistency?
?
The passage teaches that impossibility is either necessary for the essence or not—and that this determines whether it can change. Pause now and examine your own heart: are there things you have assumed are impossible for you to change—like bad habits or weak faith—that you have treated as essential to your nature? According to this lesson, if they are not essential, they could change. What would it take for you to stop treating them as permanent?

Rest as a Positive Attribute and Circular Reasoning
Razi challenges the claim that rest requires a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether rest (sukun) is a positive attribute that requires a cause, and the charge of circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Rest, if positive, requires a cause only if temporal, and proving its temporality leads to circularity.

The second objection deals with the nature of rest. The author argues that even if rest is considered a positive attribute, the need for a cause arises from temporality, not from being positive. Since the opponent cannot prove rest is temporal without assuming it needs a cause, the argument becomes circular.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully.
2
Identify the twofold denial
Razi first denies that rest is positive, then even grants it but denies it needs a cause.
Notice the concessive structure: 'لا نسلم ... سلمناه لكن لا نسلم'.
3
Understand the logical principle
The need for a cause is based on temporality (huduth), not on being a positive attribute.
4
Recognize the circularity
To prove rest needs a cause, one must show it is temporal; but to show it is temporal, one already assumes it needs a cause.
This is a classic circular argument (دور).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله امتناع الحركة إما أن يكون لازما للماهية أو لا يكون قلنا الامتناع عدم فلا يعلل، سلمنا كونه معللا لكنه لازم ووارد عليكم أيضا فإن العالم يمتنع أن يكون أزليا فهذا الامتناع إن كان لازما للماهية وجب أن يبقى ممتنعا أبدا وإن لم يكن لازما كان هذا اعترافا بحراز كون العالم أزليا وذاك يبطل قولكم . أما الوجه الثاني فنقول : لا نسلم كون السكون وصفا ثبوتيا سلمناه لكن لا نسلم افتقاره إلى المؤثر لان علة الحاجة عندكم الحدوث فلا يمكنكم بيان افتقار هذا السكون إلى المؤثر إلا إذا بينتم حدوثه وأنتم فرعتم حدوثه على هذه المقدمة فيصير ديرا . سلمناه لكن لا نسلم أن القديم لا ينعدم، فإن الله تعالى تاير من الأزل إلى الأبد على إيجاد العالم ، فبعد أن أوجه ما بقيت تلك القادرية لأن إيجاد الموجود محال ، فقد عدم ذلك التعلق القديم . وأيضا لا يقال إنه سبحانه وتعالى قادر على إيجاده بواسطة أن يعدمه ثم يعيده مرة أخرى ، لانا نقول كلامنا في إثبات ذلك النحلق المخصوص أعنى تعلق قدرته بإيجاد العالم ابتداء وهذا الذي ذكرتموه تعلق آخر ، ينتقض بأن الله تعالى كان عالما في الأزل بأن العالم معدوم ، فإذا أوجده فقد زال ذلك العلم القديم . والجواب عن الأول : أنه لا بداية لا مكان حدوث العالم ، لكن لا يلزم منه صحة كون العالم أزايا ، كما أنا إذا أخذنا هذا الحادث بشرط كونه مسبوقا لعدم سبقا زمانيا فإنه لا أول لصحة وجيده مع هذا الشرط وإلا فسينتهي في فرض المقدم إلى حيث لو وجد قبله بلحظة صار أزليا وذلك محال، ثم على فرض أنه لابداية لهذه الصحة لم يلزم صحة كونه أزليا لما أن الأزلية وسبق العدم بالزمان لا يجتمعان فكذلك . وعن الثاني : أن تقدم عدم العالم على وجوده وتقدم وجود الله تعالى على وجود العالم عندنا كنقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض عندكم ، وكما أن ذلك التقدم ليس بالزمان وإلا لزم التسلسل فكذا هذا . وعن الثالث : أنا إذا فرضنا جوهرين متميزين متماسين فنعنى بالسكون بقاءهما على هذا الوجه وبالحركة أن لا تبقى تلك المماسة بل يصير مماما لشيء آخر وعلى هذا التفسير لا حاجة إلى بيان ماهية المكان .
He said: The impossibility of motion is either necessary for the essence or not. We said: Impossibility is non-existence, so it is not caused. We grant that it is caused, but it is necessary, and the same objection applies to you: the world cannot be eternal. If this impossibility is necessary for the essence, then it must remain impossible forever; if it is not necessary, then this is an acknowledgment of the fact that the world is eternal, and that refutes your statement. As for the second aspect, we say: We do not grant that rest (sukun) is a positive attribute. We grant it, but we do not grant that it needs an effecter, because according to you the cause of need is temporality (huduth). So you cannot demonstrate that this rest needs an effecter unless you demonstrate its temporality, and you have based its temporality on this premise, resulting in circularity. We grant it, but we do not grant that the eternal does not cease to exist, for God Almighty is [intending?] from eternity to eternity to bring the world into existence. After He brings it into existence, that power remains [valid?] because bringing into existence what already exists is impossible, so that eternal relation has ceased. Also, it cannot be said that He is able to bring it into existence by means of annihilating it and then recreating it, for we say: our discussion is about proving that specific relation, namely the relation of His power to bring the world into existence initially, and what you mentioned is another relation. It is refuted by the fact that God knew in eternity that the world was non-existent; when He brings it into existence, that eternal knowledge vanishes. The answer to the first: There is no beginning to the possibility of the world's occurrence in time, but it does not imply the world is truly eternal. For if we take this temporal event with the condition that it is preceded by non-existence not in a temporal sense, then there is no beginning to the validity of its existence with this condition; otherwise it would end in a premise such that if it existed a moment earlier it would be eternal, which is impossible. Then, assuming that this possibility has no beginning, it does not imply that it is eternal, because eternality and temporal precedence of non-existence are incompatible. As for the second: The precedence of the world's non-existence over its existence and the precedence of God's existence over the world's existence is, according to us, like the precedence of some parts of time over others according to you. And just as that precedence is not temporal (otherwise it would lead to infinite regress), so too here. As for the third: If we suppose two distinct, contiguous substances, we mean by rest the preservation of their [relative] state, and by motion the non-preservation of that contiguity such that [one] becomes contiguous with something else. On this interpretation, there is no need to define the nature of place.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage addresses multiple objections. The first objection (قوله امتناع الحركة) concerns the impossibility of motion: if it is essential to the body, then it must always be impossible; if not essential, then it can change. The author responds that impossibility is a privation (adam) and thus not caused, but even if caused, the same logic applies to the opponent's claim that the world cannot be eternal. The second objection (أما الوجه الثاني) deals with rest as a positive attribute requiring a cause. The author argues that even if rest is positive, the need for a cause arises from temporality, so the opponent cannot prove rest's temporality without circularity. The third objection concerns the non-annihilation of the eternal; the author denies that the eternal cannot cease, using God's power as an example: the relation of power to bring the world into existence ceases after creation. He also addresses an objection about re-creation and the loss of eternal knowledge. Then he provides three answers: (1) the possibility of the world's temporal occurrence has no beginning but that does not imply eternal actuality; (2) the precedence of non-existence over existence is non-temporal; (3) motion and rest can be defined without reference to place, using the concept of contiguity between substances.

Key Terms
السكون
الوصف الثبوتي
المؤثر
الحدوث
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any positive attribute automatically requires a cause.
Correction: Only temporal positive attributes require a cause; eternal attributes are self-sufficient.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that even if rest is positive, it doesn't need a cause?
A) Because rest is not a real attribute
B) Because the need for cause comes from temporality
C) Because rest is the absence of motion
D) Because God is the cause of everything

Key Takeaway
The need for a cause is not inherent in positive attributes but in temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend a belief you hold, do you ever rely on circular reasoning without realizing it? How can this lesson help you be more honest in your own thinking before God?
?
This argument shows that not every 'positive' thing needs a cause. Is there an area of your life where you assume something needs a cause or explanation, when really it is part of God's eternal nature? How does that affect your worship?
?
Circular reasoning can be a subtle form of spiritual deception. When have you used a flawed argument to convince yourself or others about a matter of faith? Will you commit to examining your reasons more carefully, even if it means admitting uncertainty?

The Eternal Can Cease and Responses to Three Objections
Razi defends the idea that eternal attributes can cease, and offers three responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternal can cease to exist, as illustrated by divine attributes, and the responses to three related objections.

Core Idea
Eternal things can cease if they are relations, and the precedence of non-existence is non-temporal.

The third objection claims that the eternal cannot cease to exist. Imam Razi refutes this by noting that God's power to create is a relation that ceases after creation. He then addresses three objections: (1) about the possibility of the world's temporality, (2) about the precedence of non-existence, and (3) about defining motion and rest without place.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: denial, examples, then three answers.
2
Understand the denial and examples
Razi denies that the eternal cannot cease. He gives two examples: God's power relation and God's knowledge of non-existence.
These are relations that cease when their objects change.
3
Study the first answer
The possibility of the world's temporal occurrence has no beginning, but that does not make it eternal because eternality and temporal precedence are incompatible.
4
Study the second answer
The precedence of non-existence over existence is non-temporal, analogous to precedence of parts of time.
5
Study the third answer
Motion and rest can be defined by contiguity of substances without reference to place.

Source Text

Three Objections on Annihilation of the Eternal and Their Answers
Author refutes three objections to eternal persistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical theologians defended the persistence of eternal attributes against objections involving relational change in divine power and knowledge.

Core Idea
Relational changes in God's actions do not entail change in His essence.

This passage tackles a critical theological puzzle: if God is eternal and His attributes are eternal, how can we say that He creates the world without implying that His power or knowledge changes? The author presents three objections and their answers, employing nuanced distinctions about relations and temporal priority. Read carefully to see how the arguments unfold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to locate the three objections: the first about the power relation ceasing, the second about a potential counter-argument (annihilation and recreation), and the third about divine knowledge changing.
The objections are not explicitly numbered in the text but can be discerned from the answers.
2
Analyze the first answer
Focus on the first answer: why does the possibility of the world having no beginning not imply it is eternal? Understand the condition of being preceded by non-existence temporally.
This answer uses a logical argument about the impossibility of the world becoming eternal if it existed an instant earlier.
3
Examine the second and third answers
The second answer addresses the knowledge objection by reinterpreting priority as non-temporal. The third answer defines motion and rest to avoid ambiguity about place.
The third answer may seem unrelated but it ties to the overall defense of divine immutability.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how these answers safeguard God's immutability while allowing for creation. Note that relational changes do not affect God's essence.
This is a key distinction in Islamic theology (kalam).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمناه لكن لا نسلم أن القديم لا ينعدم، فإن الله تعالى [قادر] من الأزل إلى الأبد على إيجاد العالم ، فبعد أن أوجده ما بقيت تلك [القدرة] لأن إيجاد الموجود محال ، فقد عدم ذلك التعلق القديم . وأيضا لا يقال إنه سبحانه وتعالى قادر على إيجاده بواسطة أن يعدمه ثم يعيده مرة أخرى ، لانا نقول كلامنا في إثبات ذلك التعلق المخصوص أعنى تعلق قدرته بإيجاد العالم ابتداء وهذا الذي ذكرتموه تعلق آخر ، ينتقض بأن الله تعالى كان عالما في الأزل بأن العالم معدوم ، فإذا أوجده فقد زال ذلك العلم القديم . والجواب عن الأول : أنه لا بداية لامكان حدوث العالم ، لكن لا يلزم منه صحة كون العالم أزليا ، كما أنا إذا أخذنا هذا الحادث بشرط كونه مسبوقا لعدم سبقا زمانيا فإنه لا أول لصحة وجوده مع هذا الشرط وإلا فسينتهي في فرض المقدم إلى حيث لو وجد قبله بلحظة صار أزليا وذلك محال، ثم على فرض أنه لابداية لهذه الصحة لم يلزم صحة كونه أزليا لما أن الأزلية وسبق العدم بالزمان لا يجتمعان فكذلك . وعن الثاني : أن تقدم عدم العالم على وجوده وتقدم وجود الله تعالى على وجود العالم عندنا كتقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض عندكم ، وكما أن ذلك التقدم ليس بالزمان وإلا لزم التسلسل فكذا هذا . وعن الثالث : أنا إذا فرضنا جوهرين متميزين متماسين فنعنى بالسكون بقاءهما على هذا الوجه وبالحركة أن لا تبقى تلك المماسة بل يصير مماسا لشيء آخر وعلى هذا التفسير لا حاجة إلى بيان ماهية المكان .
We concede it, but we do not concede that the eternal cannot cease to exist. For God Almighty is [powerful] from eternity to perpetuity to bring the world into existence. So after He brings it into existence, that [power] does not remain, because bringing an existent into existence is impossible, so that eternal relation has ceased. And also it cannot be said that He, glorified and exalted, is powerful to bring it into existence by means of annihilating it and then recreating it, for we say our discussion is about the proof of that specific relation, namely the relation of His power to bring the world into existence originally, and what you have mentioned is another relation. It is objected that God Almighty was eternally knowing that the world is non-existent, so when He brings it into existence, that eternal knowledge has passed away. The answer to the first: There is no beginning for the possibility of the world’s origination, but it does not necessitate the soundness of the world being eternal, just as when we take this temporal thing with the condition of being preceded by non-existence in a temporal sense, there is no beginning for the soundness of its existence with this condition. Otherwise, it would lead in the assumption of the premise to a point where if it existed an instant earlier, it would become eternal, and that is impossible. Then, assuming there is no beginning for this soundness, it does not necessitate the soundness of its being eternal, because eternity and temporal priority of non-existence are incompatible. The answer to the second: The priority of the world’s non-existence over its existence and the priority of God’s existence over the world’s existence according to us is like the priority of some parts of time over others according to you, and just as that priority is not temporal—else it would lead to infinite regress—so too this. The answer to the third: If we suppose two distinct adjoining substances, by rest we mean their remaining in this state, and by motion we mean that the contact does not remain but it becomes contiguous to something else. On this interpretation, there is no need to explain the essence of place.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage contains three objections and their answers. The first objection: if the eternal cannot cease, how can the relation of God's power to create the world cease after creation? The author answers that the possibility of the world's origination has no beginning (it is always possible), but that does not mean the world itself is eternal; the condition of being preceded by non-exclusion temporally does not imply an infinite past. The second objection: how can God's knowledge change? The author answers by analogy: the priority of God's existence over the world is like the priority of one time part over another, which is not temporal, thus no change in God. The third objection: by defining rest and motion in terms of contact and separation, there is no need to define place, thus avoiding a circularity. The responses rely on distinguishing between eternal attributes and their temporal effects.

Key Terms
قديم
تعلق القدرة
تعلق العلم
أزلية
حدوث
تقدم زماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that cessation of a relation (e.g., God's power to create) implies a change in God's essence.
Correction: The author argues that only the relation changes, not God's attribute itself; the power remains but its specific relational aspect ceases.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection about God's eternal knowledge ceasing?
A) God's knowledge is not eternal.
B) The priority of God's existence to the world is not temporal, so no change in God.
C) God's knowledge is created.
D) The world never existed.

Key Takeaway
Relational changes in God's actions (like creation) do not alter His eternal attributes; they are merely new relations to temporal things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that God's power to create does not diminish after creation. Does your heart truly believe that God's power is still fully 'available' to help you in every moment, or do you unconsciously think He is 'used up' after past acts? Will you stop limiting His power in your mind?
?
The passage says God's knowledge of your deeds never changes—it was always there eternally. Does that thought terrify you or comfort you? If you have sins you hide, will you still try to hide from Him, knowing He already knew them before you committed them?
?
God's relation to the world is non-temporal: He does not 'react' to your actions. How does this challenge your feeling that you can 'surprise' God with your good deeds? Are you willing to accept that your worship is known from eternity, and still worship with sincerity?
