The Fourth Objection: God's Purpose and Perfection
Razi presents the fourth objection and the incomplete answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against God being a free agent and the initial response that merely asserts divine free agency.

Core Idea
A free agent acting for a purpose seems to imply need—can God have a purpose?

This lesson examines a classic objection in Islamic theology: if God is a free agent (mukhtar), then His actions must have a purpose (ghaya), but a purpose would imply that God is perfected by the act, contradicting His absolute perfection. The objection challenges the very notion of divine free agency. We'll see the objection and the initial answer that merely restates that God is free.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Begin by reading the Arabic passage: the claim that a free agent acts for a purpose by which the agent is perfected, and that this is problematic for God.
Note the phrase 'فعل مختار يكون لغاية يستعمل بها الفاعل' – the act of a free agent is for a purpose that perfects the agent.
2
Analyze the initial answer
The passage says the author only answered by saying 'We will show that the agent is free.' Why might this answer be considered insufficient?
Think about how simply asserting free agency doesn't resolve the problem of purpose implying need.
3
Connect to broader debate
Consider why this objection matters for the proof of the world's origination (huduth). The lesson sets the stage for the positions that follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتشكيك الرابع : بأن فعل المختار يكون لغاية يستعمل بها الفاعل وذلك في حق الله تعالى محل ، فلم يجب عنه إلا بقوله إنا سنبين أن الفاعل مختار . والجواب الصحيح على رأى بعض المتكلمين : أن الغاية هناك استكمال الفعل لا الفاعل وعلى رأى بعضهم لاغاية هناك، وعند الفلاسفة أن الغاية هناك نفس الفاعل لأنه تعالى إنما يفعل لذلك ولأنه فوق الكمال، فهذا أورده المصنف. والكلام فيه وعليه في هذا الباب وبقى علينا أن نذكر ما هو الصحيح مما قالوه في مسألة الحدوث فيقول : أنه الدليل الذي اعتمد عليه جمهور المتكلمين في هذه المسألة يحتاج إلى إقامة حجة على دعوى واحدة من الدعاوى
And the fourth objection: that the act of a free agent (mukhtar) is for a purpose by which the agent is perfected, and that is problematic in the case of God. So he did not answer it except by saying: 'We will show that the agent is free.' And the correct answer according to some theologians (mutakallimun) is that the purpose there is the perfection of the act, not the agent; and according to some, there is no purpose there; and according to the philosophers, the purpose there is the agent Himself, for He only acts for the sake of being, and because He is above perfection. This is what the author (musannif) presented. The discourse about it and against it is in this chapter, and it remains for us to mention what is correct among what they said regarding the issue of origination (huduth). He says: 'The proof upon which the majority of the theologians relied in this issue needs to establish an argument for one of the claims.'

Commentary
This passage deals with the fourth objection: if God is a free agent, His action must have a purpose, which would imply that He perfects Himself through the act, contradicting His perfection. The author notes that the initial answer (promising to show God is free) is insufficient. He then presents three positions: (1) Some theologians say the purpose is the perfection of the act itself, not the agent. (2) Others say there is no purpose at all. (3) The philosophers say the purpose is God Himself, as He acts for the sake of being and is beyond perfection. The author concludes that the correct answer will be discussed further, and he transitions to the next topic: the proof relied upon by most theologians and its need for a proper argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God has a purpose, it necessarily implies that He is imperfect or needs something.
Correction: The objector assumes that a purpose always perfects the agent, but later positions will show that purpose can be for the act itself or for God's essence without implying need.

Checkpoint
What is the core problem raised in the fourth objection?
A) God cannot act freely because He has no hands.
B) A free agent's act is for a purpose that perfects the agent, but God is already perfect.
C) God's act has no purpose at all.
D) The free agent's act is not real.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection challenges divine free agency by arguing that a purpose implies need, and the initial answer merely reiterating free agency is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What does this passage reveal about the danger of superficial answers? Will you commit to honestly facing difficult questions about your faith, even when the answers are incomplete?
?
When you do good deeds, do you sometimes do them with a hidden need for validation or personal growth? How does the idea of God acting without any need challenge your own motives? Can you perform an act of worship today solely for His sake, expecting nothing in return?

Three Positions on Divine Purpose
Razi outlines three theological and philosophical answers to the purpose objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three major positions on the purpose of God's act: perfection of the act, no purpose, and the agent Himself as purpose.

Core Idea
God's purpose can be the act's perfection, no purpose at all, or His own essence.

After the objection, Imam Razi presents three answers from different schools of thought. Some theologians say the purpose is the perfection of the act itself, not the agent. Others deny any purpose altogether. The philosophers hold that the purpose is God Himself, since He acts for the sake of being. This lesson explores these positions and their implications, leading to the transition to the proof of origination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the Arabic passage and note each position: (1) purpose is perfection of the act, (2) no purpose, (3) purpose is the agent Himself.
Look for the phrases: 'استكمال الفعل لا الفاعل', 'لا غاية هناك', 'نفس الفاعل'.
2
Understand each position's reasoning
For each, consider the underlying rationale. Why would a theologian say the act itself is perfected? Why would another deny purpose? Why do philosophers say God Himself is the purpose?
The philosophers' view is that God acts for the sake of being (لذلك) and because He is above perfection (فوق الكمال).
3
Reflect on the correct answer
The author mentions that the discourse on these positions will continue, and then transitions to the need for a proper argument in the proof of origination. Think about which position might be theologically sound.
This sets up the next topic: the proof of huduth.

The Purpose of God's Actions: Objection and Answers
Author presents the fourth doubt and three theological responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against attributing a purpose to God's actions and the three main theological responses to it.

Core Idea
God's actions have no deficiency to be perfected; the purpose lies in the act itself.

This lesson examines a key objection raised against the doctrine that God is a free agent. Critics argue that any free action aims at a purpose that perfects the agent, which seems incompatible with God's perfection. We will explore how theologians and philosophers answered this challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and identify the fourth doubt: that a free agent acts for a purpose, and this seems inappropriate for God.
Notice the phrase 'يكون لغاية'.
2
Examine the three answers
The passage presents three distinct answers: (1) some theologians say purpose is the perfection of the act; (2) others say there is no purpose; (3) philosophers say the purpose is God Himself.
Each answer maintains God's transcendence in a different way.
3
Reflect on the correct view
The author notes that further discussion is needed. Consider which answer you find most convincing and why.
This prepares you for the next lesson on al-ḥudūth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتشكيك الرابع : بأن فعل المختار يكون لغاية يستعمل بها الفاعل وذلك في حق الله تعالى محل ، فلم يجب عنه إلا بقوله إنا سنبين أن الفاعل مختار . والجواب الصحيح على رأى بعض المتكلمين : أن الغاية هناك استكمال الفعل لا الفاعل وعلى رأى بعضهم لاغاية هناك، وعند الفلاسفة أن الغاية هناك نفس الفاعل لأنه تعالى إنما يفعل لذلك ولأنه فوق الكمال ، فهذا أورده المصنف. والكلام فيه وعليه في هذا الباب وبقى علينا أن نذكر ما هو الصحيح مما قالوه في مسألة الحدوث
And the fourth doubt: that the action of the free agent (al-mukhtār) is for a purpose by which the agent is [؟] and that is [؟] with respect to God Most High. So he did not answer it except by saying 'We will show that the agent is free.' And the correct answer according to some theologians (al-mutakallimīn): that the purpose there is the perfection of the act, not the agent; and according to some others, there is no purpose there; and according to the philosophers, the purpose there is the agent Himself, because He Most High only acts for that and because He is above perfection. This is what the author mentioned. And the discussion about it and against it is in this chapter, and it remains for us to mention what is correct from what they said regarding the issue of origination (al-ḥudūth).
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection: A free agent acts for a purpose that benefits the agent, but this is inappropriate for God (since He is perfect and self-sufficient). The initial answer only asserts that God is free (mukhtār). Then the author gives three more detailed answers: (1) Some theologians say the purpose is the perfection of the act itself, not the agent—so God acts to make the act perfect. (2) Others say there is no purpose at all; God acts without any goal. (3) The philosophers say the purpose is God Himself, because He acts for His own essence, and He is above perfection (i.e., He is already perfect, so His action simply emanates from His essence). The author notes he has only summarized what various groups say, and further discussion is needed to determine the correct position on the issue of the world's origination (ḥudūth).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God acts for a purpose that benefits Him.
Correction: The correct view is that the purpose is in the perfection of the act, or there is no purpose, or the purpose is God Himself, all of which avoid attributing need to God.

The fourth doubt about God's purpose is answered by shifting the purpose away from the agent to the act, negating purpose altogether, or identifying it with God's essence.

?
If God's essence is the ultimate purpose of His actions, what then is the purpose of your life? Can you honestly say that your actions are aimed at God's essence alone, without any trace of seeking recognition or worldly gain?

Annihilation by Agent and the Occurrence of a Contrary
Refutation of the existential/non-existential dilemma and proposal of annihilation by a contrary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to annihilation by the agent's act and the proposed alternative of annihilation through the occurrence of a contrary.

Core Idea
Annihilation cannot be a new act; it must occur through a contrary.

The Karamiyya argued that if God annihilates, His act must be either existential or non-existential — both problematic. Imam al-Razi responds by showing the flaw in this division and then introduces another possibility: annihilation by the occurrence of a contrary. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how the opponent sets up the dilemma.
The opponent tries to force a choice between annihilation being a positive act or pure non-existence.
2
Understand the dilemma
The opponent says: if annihilation is existential, it becomes a new being, not non-existence. If not, it is nothing — then nothing happens. This seems to deny any annihilation.
3
Follow Razi's response
Razi grants the invalidity of that division but asks: why not annihilation by a contrary? This shifts the ground.
A contrary (like white vs black) naturally removes the other without needing a separate act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثلاثة الأول ما تقدم في مسألة الحدوث وعن الرابع أن نقول لم لا يجوز أن يعدم بإعدام الفاعل. قوله الإعدام إما أن يكون أمرا وجوديا أو لا يكون قلنا يقتضى أن لا يعدم شيء البتة، لأنه يقال إذا عدم الشيء فهل يتجدد أمر أم لا يتجدد ، فإن لم يتجدد أمر فهو لم يعدم ، وإن تجدد فالمتجدد عدم أو وجود ، لا جائز أن يكون عدما لأنه لا فرق بين أن يقال لم يتجدد وبين أن يقال تجدد العدم وإلا فأحد المعدمين يخالف الآخر وهو محاله وإن كان وجودها كان حدوثاً لموجود آخر لا عدما للوجود الأول ، سلمنا فساد هذا القسم فلم لا يجوز أن يفنى بحدوث الضد. وقوله في الوجه الأول : حدوث الحادث متوقف على عدم الباقى قلت لا نسلم فإن عندنا عدم الباقى معلول الحادث ، والعلة وإن امتنع انفكاكها عن المعلول لكن لا حاجة بها إلى المعلول . قوله في الوجه الثاني : المضادة حاصلة من الجانبين ، قلنا لم لا يجوز أن يكون الحادث أقوى لحدوثه وإن كنا لا نعرف لمية كون الحدوث سلبا للقوة سلبنا فساد هذا القسم لكن لم لا يجوز أن يعدم الجسم لانتفاء الشرط ، بيانه هو أن العرض لا يبقى والجوهر ممتنع الخلو عندنا ، فإذا لم يخلق الله تعالى العرض انتفى الجوهر . قوله : يلزم الدور قلنا لم لا يجوز أن يقال الجوهر والعرض يتلازمان وإن لم يكن لأحدهما حاجة إلى الآخر كما في المتضادين ومعلولى العلة الواحدة فإذا لم يوجد أحد المتلازمين وجب عدم الآخر
The answer to the first three [points] has preceded in the issue of temporality (al-ḥudūth). As for the fourth, we say: why is it not permissible for it to become non-existent by the annihilation of the agent? He says: annihilation is either existential or not. We say: it entails that nothing becomes non-existent at all, because when a thing becomes non-existent, does something new occur or not? If nothing new occurs, then it has not become non-existent. If something new occurs, then what occurs is either non-existence or existence. It cannot be non-existence because there is no difference between saying 'nothing new occurred' and saying 'non-existence occurred'—otherwise one of the two annihilations would be different from the other, which is impossible. And if it is existence, then it is the occurrence of another existent, not a non-existence of the first existence. We grant the invalidity of this division; then why is it not permissible for it to perish by the occurrence of a contrary? And his statement in the first point: the occurrence of the new depends on the non-existence of the enduring. I say: we do not accept this, because in our view the non-existence of the enduring is an effect of the new; and although the cause cannot be separated from the effect, it does not need the effect. His statement in the second point: contrariety exists on both sides. We say: why is it not permissible for the new to be stronger due to its occurrence, even if we do not know the reason why occurrence is a privation of strength? We grant the invalidity of this division; but why is it not permissible for the body to become non-existent due to the removal of a condition? Its elucidation is: the accident does not persist, and the substance is impossible to be devoid [of accidents] according to us. So if God did not create the accident, the substance ceases to exist. He says: this leads to a vicious circle. We say: why is it not permissible to say that the substance and accident are interdependent, even if one has no need of the other—like two contraries or two effects of one cause? So if one of the two interdependent things does not exist, the other necessarily ceases to exist.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage provides responses to additional objections to the Karamiyya's proof. The first response concerns the fourth possibility (annihilation by the act of the agent). The opponent argued that annihilation is either existential or not; if existential, it is a new being, not non-existence; if not existential, it is pure non-existence and cannot be attributed to God. The answer grants the invalidity of that division but asks why annihilation cannot occur through the occurrence of a contrary (the second division). Then it addresses two specific objections. First, the objection that the occurrence of a new thing depends on the non-existence of the enduring is rejected: the non-existence of the enduring is an effect of the new, not a precondition. Second, concerning the notion that contrariety is mutual, the answer suggests that the new may be stronger by virtue of its novelty, even if the reason is unknown. Finally, the possibility of annihilation by removal of a condition is defended: accidents do not persist (they are constantly recreated), and if God does not create an accident, the substance (which cannot exist without accidents) ceases. The charge of a vicious circle is rebutted by proposing that substance and accident may be interdependent without causal priority, like two effects of a single cause or two mutually exclusive contraries.

Key Terms
إعدام الفاعل
الضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Annihilation must be directly caused by an act that is either a positive entity or pure non-existence.
Correction: Annihilation can be indirectly achieved through the occurrence of a contrary, without requiring a separate existential act.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why is it impossible for non-existence to occur as a new event?
A) Because non-existence cannot be created by God
B) Because there is no difference between saying 'nothing new occurred' and 'non-existence occurred'
C) Because non-existence would require an existing entity
D) Because the opponent does not accept non-existence

Key Takeaway
Annihilation by a contrary avoids the dilemma of requiring an existential or non-existential act, and shows that God's power can be manifest through natural opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that one thing can cease to exist simply because its contrary appears. In your own life, what harmful belief or action might you be able to eliminate by intentionally cultivating its opposite? How will you start today?
?
The opponent in the passage thought annihilation must be either a positive act or nothing at all. Have you ever felt that changing yourself requires either a dramatic effort or is impossible? How does the concept of 'annihilation by contrary' offer a third path for personal transformation? What small 'contrary' can you introduce this week to replace a bad habit?

Responses to Objections on Dependency and Contrariety
Refutation of two objections to annihilation by a contrary, leading to the suggestion of annihilation by removal of condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two objections against annihilation by a contrary: that the new depends on the non-existence of the old, and that contrariety is mutual — and how Razi refutes them.

Core Idea
The new does not depend on the non-existence of the old; rather, the non-existence is an effect.

After proposing annihilation by a contrary, Razi faces two objections. The first claims the new's occurrence depends on the old's non-existence. The second says contrariety works both ways. Razi provides concise refutations and then moves to another possible mechanism: annihilation by removal of condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the two objections and the responses.
The first objection is about dependency, the second about mutual contrariety.
2
Understand the first objection and response
The opponent says: the new thing coming into existence depends on the non-existence of the enduring thing. Razi counters: the non-existence of the enduring is an effect of the new, not a precondition.
This reverses the causal order: the new thing causes the old to vanish, not the other way around.
3
Understand the second objection and response
The opponent says contrariety is mutual — each opposite is equally opposed to the other. Razi suggests the new may be stronger due to its novelty, even if we don't know the mechanism.
4
Note the transition to removal of condition
After granting the invalidity of the second division, Razi asks: why not annihilation by removal of condition? This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثلاثة الأول ما تقدم في مسألة الحدوث وعن الرابع أن نقول لم لا يجوز أن يعدم بإعدام الفاعل. قوله الإعدام إما أن يكون أمرا وجوديا أو لا يكون قلنا يقتضى أن لا يعدم شيء البتة، لأنه يقال إذا عدم الشيء فهل يتجدد أمر أم لا يتجدد ، فإن لم يتجدد أمر فهو لم يعدم ، وإن تجدد فالمتجدد عدم أو وجود ، لا جائز أن يكون عدما لأنه لا فرق بين أن يقال لم يتجدد وبين أن يقال تجدد العدم وإلا فأحد المعدمين يخالف الآخر وهو محاله وإن كان وجودها كان حدوثاً لموجود آخر لا عدما للوجود الأول ، سلمنا فساد هذا القسم فلم لا يجوز أن يفنى بحدوث الضد. وقوله في الوجه الأول : حدوث الحادث متوقف على عدم الباقى قلت لا نسلم فإن عندنا عدم الباقى معلول الحادث ، والعلة وإن امتنع انفكاكها عن المعلول لكن لا حاجة بها إلى المعلول . قوله في الوجه الثاني : المضادة حاصلة من الجانبين ، قلنا لم لا يجوز أن يكون الحادث أقوى لحدوثه وإن كنا لا نعرف لمية كون الحدوث سلبا للقوة سلبنا فساد هذا القسم لكن لم لا يجوز أن يعدم الجسم لانتفاء الشرط ، بيانه هو أن العرض لا يبقى والجوهر ممتنع الخلو عندنا ، فإذا لم يخلق الله تعالى العرض انتفى الجوهر . قوله : يلزم الدور قلنا لم لا يجوز أن يقال الجوهر والعرض يتلازمان وإن لم يكن لأحدهما حاجة إلى الآخر كما في المتضادين ومعلولى العلة الواحدة فإذا لم يوجد أحد المتلازمين وجب عدم الآخر
The answer to the first three [points] has preceded in the issue of temporality (al-ḥudūth). As for the fourth, we say: why is it not permissible for it to become non-existent by the annihilation of the agent? He says: annihilation is either existential or not. We say: it entails that nothing becomes non-existent at all, because when a thing becomes non-existent, does something new occur or not? If nothing new occurs, then it has not become non-existent. If something new occurs, then what occurs is either non-existence or existence. It cannot be non-existence because there is no difference between saying 'nothing new occurred' and saying 'non-existence occurred'—otherwise one of the two annihilations would be different from the other, which is impossible. And if it is existence, then it is the occurrence of another existent, not a non-existence of the first existence. We grant the invalidity of this division; then why is it not permissible for it to perish by the occurrence of a contrary? And his statement in the first point: the occurrence of the new depends on the non-existence of the enduring. I say: we do not accept this, because in our view the non-existence of the enduring is an effect of the new; and although the cause cannot be separated from the effect, it does not need the effect. His statement in the second point: contrariety exists on both sides. We say: why is it not permissible for the new to be stronger due to its occurrence, even if we do not know the reason why occurrence is a privation of strength? We grant the invalidity of this division; but why is it not permissible for the body to become non-existent due to the removal of a condition? Its elucidation is: the accident does not persist, and the substance is impossible to be devoid [of accidents] according to us. So if God did not create the accident, the substance ceases to exist. He says: this leads to a vicious circle. We say: why is it not permissible to say that the substance and accident are interdependent, even if one has no need of the other—like two contraries or two effects of one cause? So if one of the two interdependent things does not exist, the other necessarily ceases to exist.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage provides responses to additional objections to the Karamiyya's proof. The first response concerns the fourth possibility (annihilation by the act of the agent). The opponent argued that annihilation is either existential or not; if existential, it is a new being, not non-existence; if not existential, it is pure non-existence and cannot be attributed to God. The answer grants the invalidity of that division but asks why annihilation cannot occur through the occurrence of a contrary (the second division). Then it addresses two specific objections. First, the objection that the occurrence of a new thing depends on the non-existence of the enduring is rejected: the non-existence of the enduring is an effect of the new, not a precondition. Second, concerning the notion that contrariety is mutual, the answer suggests that the new may be stronger by virtue of its novelty, even if the reason is unknown. Finally, the possibility of annihilation by removal of a condition is defended: accidents do not persist (they are constantly recreated), and if God does not create an accident, the substance (which cannot exist without accidents) ceases. The charge of a vicious circle is rebutted by proposing that substance and accident may be interdependent without causal priority, like two effects of a single cause or two mutually exclusive contraries.

Key Terms
التوقف
الضد
المضادة
العلة
المعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The new thing coming into existence requires the prior non-existence of its opposite as a condition.
Correction: The non-existence of the opposite is an effect of the new existence, not a precondition. The causal order is reversed.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's response to the objection that the occurrence of a new thing depends on the non-existence of the enduring?
A) He agrees and uses it to support annihilation by removal
B) He says the non-existence of the enduring is an effect of the new
C) He says the new cannot occur without the non-existence
D) He denies that the non-existence occurs at all

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes dependency and mutual contrariety objections, then pivots to a new mechanism: annihilation by removal of condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to let go of a sin, do you assume that its non-existence must come before you can embrace righteousness? How does Razi's argument that non-existence is an effect, not a precondition, challenge you to simply turn to God and let the old fall away as a consequence? Will you trust that your repentance itself can cause the old to vanish, rather than waiting for it to disappear first?
?
The objection assumed that contraries are equally strong. In your spiritual life, do you feel trapped by the belief that your bad habits are just as powerful as your good intentions? Razi suggests that the new can be stronger simply because it is new. Will you today claim the strength of a fresh start with God, confident that His new mercy outweighs the weight of your past failures?

Annihilation by Removal of Condition and Substance-Accident Interdependence
Defense of annihilation by removal of a condition and rebuttal of the charge of vicious circle through interdependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how annihilation can occur through removal of a condition, and how the interdependence of substance and accident avoids a vicious circle.

Core Idea
If God does not create the accident, the substance ceases to exist — no circularity is involved.

The final argument in this section proposes that God can annihilate a body by simply not creating its sustaining accident. Since accidents do not persist and a substance cannot exist without any accident, withholding the accident suffices. The objection of a vicious circle is answered by appealing to mutual interdependence without causal priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the key claims: accidents do not persist, substance cannot be devoid of accidents.
2
Understand the mechanism
If God does not create an accident for a substance at a given moment, the substance necessarily ceases to exist because it requires an accident to exist.
This is based on the Ash'ari view that accidents are constantly recreated.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The opponent says this leads to a circle: substance depends on accident, accident depends on substance. Razi replies that they can be interdependent like two effects of one cause or two contraries.
Interdependence does not imply a vicious circle if there is no causal priority.

Philosophers' Order of Elements
Philosophers describe the order of the four elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim about the concentric order of the four elements and the role of motion in determining their thermal and density properties.

Core Idea
The philosophers arranged elements in concentric spheres, with fire hottest due to proximity to the moving sphere.

In this passage, the author presents the philosophical model of the four elements. The philosophers argue that the celestial sphere's motion generates heat, so the nearest element, fire, is hottest and most subtle, while the farthest, earth, is coldest and densest. This sets the physical basis for their cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall claim. Notice the specific terms for the elements.
Note the phrase 'except water'.
2
Identify the concentric order
List the elements in order from center outward: earth, water, air, fire. Understand that each surrounds the previous except water.
The exception for water may indicate it is not a full sphere.
3
Understand the role of motion
The philosophers claim that motion of the sphere causes heat. Therefore, the closer an element is to the sphere, the hotter and subtler it is.
4
Correlate distance with properties
Fire is hottest and subtlest; earth is coldest and densest; air and water are intermediate accordingly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما العناصر : فزعموا أن الأرض محفوفة بالماء والماء بالهواء والهواء بالنار وانها كرات منطق بعضها على البعض إلا الماء وزعموا أن الحركة مسخنة فالجرم الملاصق للفلك يجب أن يكون في غاية السخونة واللطافة وهو النار والذي يكون في غاية البعد يجب أن يكون في غاية البرودة والكثافة وهو الأرض والذي يلاصق النار وهو الهواء يكون تاليا في اللطافة والذي يلحق الأرض يتلوها في الكثافة فهذا هو الوصف المحكم في ترتيب العناصر إلا أن هذا الكلام يقتضي أن يكون الأرض أبرد من الهواء وهو على خلاف قولهم وأن يكون النار في غاية الرطوبة لأن الرطوبة عندهم مفسرة بسهولة قبول الأشكال لا بسهولة الالتصاق بالتحيز ولم يكن الهواء رطبا . أقول: الحكماء لا يزعمون أن حرارة النار مقتضاة حركة الفلك بل انما قالوا انها مقتضاة صورتها وهي ذاتية وما يفيده الفلك يكون غريبا ، وإنما نقل ذلك عن قول الكبيري وأمثاله . وقد ذكر ابن سينا ذلك نقلا عنه وقال إنه كان شديد التذبذب وكذلك القول في تعليل برودة الأرض وكثافتها ببعدها عن الفلك وأما قوله هذا الكلام يقتضي أن تكون الأرض أبرد من الماء وهو على خلاف قولهم أيضا فيه نظر فإنهم لم يعللوا البرد بالبعد عن الفلك ولم يقولوا إن الأرض ليست أبرد من الماء انما قالوا الماء أبرد عند الحس وحيث قالوا الكثافة مقتضاء البرودة حكموا بأن الأرض أبرد في نفسها لكونها أكثف وقلة الإحساس بها لعدم نفوذها في المسام لكثافتها أيضا وأما الرطوبة فإن كانت مفسرة بسهولة قبول الأشكال كان النقض بالنار واردا عليهم وإن كانت سهولة القبول محمولة عليها فلا لأن المحمول ربما يكون أعم والحق أن النار مجفف وليس يابس بالمعنى المقابل للمعنى الموجود في الماء.
As for the elements: they claimed that earth is surrounded by water, water by air, air by fire, and that they are spheres enclosing each other except water, and they claimed that motion is heating, so the body adjacent to the sphere must be at the utmost heat and subtlety, and that is fire; and that which is at the utmost distance must be at the utmost cold and density, and that is earth; and that which is adjacent to fire, namely air, follows in subtlety; and that which follows earth follows it in density. This is the precise description of the order of elements. However, this statement requires that earth be colder than air, contrary to their claim, and that fire be at the utmost moisture, because moisture according to them is defined as ease of accepting shapes, not ease of adhesion to location, and air was not moist. I say: The philosophers do not claim that the heat of fire is necessitated by the motion of the sphere; rather they said it is necessitated by its form, which is essential, and what the sphere imparts is extraneous. This was transmitted from the statement of al-Kabiri and his like. Ibn Sina mentioned that as a transmission from him and said that he was very unstable. Similarly for the explanation of the coldness and density of earth by its distance from the sphere. As for his statement that this speech requires that earth be colder than water, contrary to their claim, there is consideration: they did not explain coldness by distance from the sphere, and they did not say that earth is not colder than water; rather they said water is colder by sensation, and when they said density necessitates coldness, they judged that earth is colder in itself because it is denser, and the lack of sensation of it is due to its density preventing penetration into pores. As for moisture, if it is defined as ease of accepting shapes, then the objection by fire is valid against them; but if ease of acceptance is predicated [broadly], then no, because the predicate is perhaps more general. The truth is that fire is desiccating and not dry in the sense opposite to the sense found in water.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' description of the order of the four elements, followed by an objection and a detailed response. The author first relates the philosophers' claim: earth is surrounded by water, water by air, air by fire, and these are concentric spheres except for water. Motion is heating, so the body closest to the sphere (fire) is hottest and most subtle, and the farthest (earth) is coldest and densest. Air is second in subtlety, and water second in density. The author then objects: this arrangement implies that earth is colder than air, contradicting the philosophers' own view, and that fire should be extremely moist, whereas they define moisture as ease of accepting shapes, but air is not moist. Then the author inserts a marginal note (أقول) defending the philosophers: they do not attribute fire's heat to motion but to its form; the citation from al-Kabiri is unreliable, and Ibn Sina noted his instability. Regarding the objection about coldness, the philosophers do not explain coldness by distance but by density; earth is colder in itself but not sensed due to its density. As for moisture, if defined as ease of accepting shapes, then fire is not moist, but if moisture is broader, the objection fails. The note concludes that fire is desiccating, not dry in the sense opposite to water's moisture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
They claimed motion causes heat. What spiritual 'motion' in your life generates warmth towards Allah? What activities cool your heart? How can you move closer to the source of true heat?
?
The passage presents a precise order. Are you willing to submit your own intellectual arrangements to divine order, even if they seem rational? Commit to a specific instance where you will trust revelation over your reasoning this week.

Critique of the Philosophers' Elemental Order
Objection and response regarding the philosophers' elemental order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the philosophers' elemental order and the defense offered by later thinkers, including redefinition of terms.

Core Idea
Objections about earth's temperature and fire's moisture are refuted by distinguishing essential from accidental properties and clarifying definitions.

The author now raises two objections to the philosophers' model: it implies earth is colder than air (contrary to their claim) and that fire should be extremely moist. He then responds in a marginal note, defending the philosophers by correcting a mistaken attribution to al-Kabiri and clarifying their theories of heat, cold, and moisture.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection paragraph
Read the first part of the passage that starts with 'إلا أن هذا الكلام'. Note the two implied contradictions regarding earth's temperature and fire's moisture.
2
Examine the response
The response ('أقول') addresses each point: fire's heat is from its form, not motion; earth's coldness is due to density, not distance; moisture definition depends on interpretation.
Notice the citation of Ibn Sina and al-Kabiri.
3
Understand the dialectical method
This passage shows how philosophical claims are critiqued and defended through precise definitions and source criticism.

أما العناصر : فزعموا أن الأرض محفوفة بالماء والماء بالهواء والهواء بالنار وانها كرات منطق بعضها على البعض إلا الماء وزعموا أن الحركة مسخنة فالجرم الملاصق للفلك يجب أن يكون في غاية السخونة واللطافة وهو النار والذي يكون في غاية البعد يجب أن يكون في غاية البرودة والكثافة وهو الأرض والذي يلاصق النار وهو الهواء يكون تاليا في اللطافة والذي يلحق الأرض يتلوها في الكثافة فهذا هو الوصف المحكم في ترتيب العناصر إلا أن هذا الكلام يقتضي أن يكون الأرض أبرد من الهواء وهو على خلاف قولهم وأن يكون النار في غاية الرطوبة لأن الرطوبة عندهم مفسرة بسهولة قبول الأشكال لا بسهولة الالتصاق بالتحيز ولم يكن الهواء رطبا . أقول: الحكماء لا يزعمون أن حرارة النار مقتضاة حركة الفلك بل انما قالوا انها مقتضاة صورتها وهي ذاتية وما يفيده الفلك يكون غريبا ، وإنما نقل ذلك عن قول الكبيري وأمثاله . وقد ذكر ابن سينا ذلك نقلا عنه وقال إنه كان شديد التذبذب وكذلك القول في تعليل برودة الأرض وكثافتها ببعدها عن الفلك وأما قوله هذا الكلام يقتضي أن تكون الأرض أبرد من الماء وهو على خلاف قولهم أيضا فيه نظر فإنهم لم يعللوا البرد بالبعد عن الفلك ولم يقولوا إن الأرض ليست أبرد من الماء انما قالوا الماء أبرد عند الحس وحيث قالوا الكثافة مقتضاء البرودة حكموا بأن الأرض أبرد في نفسها لكونها أكثف وقلة الإحساس بها لعدم نفوذها في المسام لكثافتها أيضا وأما الرطوبة فإن كانت مفسرة بسهولة قبول الأشكال كان النقض بالنار واردا عليهم وإن كانت سهولة القبول محمولة عليها فلا لأن المحمول ربما يكون أعم والحق أن النار مجفف وليس يابس بالمعنى المقابل للمعنى الموجود في الماء.
As for the elements: they claimed that earth is surrounded by water, water by air, air by fire, and that they are spheres enclosing each other except water, and they claimed that motion is heating, so the body adjacent to the sphere must be at the utmost heat and subtlety, and that is fire; and that which is at the utmost distance must be at the utmost cold and density, and that is earth; and that which is adjacent to fire, namely air, follows in subtlety; and that which follows earth follows it in density. This is the precise description of the order of elements. However, this statement requires that earth be colder than air, contrary to their claim, and that fire be at the utmost moisture, because moisture according to them is defined as ease of accepting shapes, not ease of adhesion to location, and air was not moist. I say: The philosophers do not claim that the heat of fire is necessitated by the motion of the sphere; rather they said it is necessitated by its form, which is essential, and what the sphere imparts is extraneous. This was transmitted from the statement of al-Kabiri and his like. Ibn Sina mentioned that as a transmission from him and said that he was very unstable. Similarly for the explanation of the coldness and density of earth by its distance from the sphere. As for his statement that this speech requires that earth be colder than water, contrary to their claim, there is consideration: they did not explain coldness by distance from the sphere, and they did not say that earth is not colder than water; rather they said water is colder by sensation, and when they said density necessitates coldness, they judged that earth is colder in itself because it is denser, and the lack of sensation of it is due to its density preventing penetration into pores. As for moisture, if it is defined as ease of accepting shapes, then the objection by fire is valid against them; but if ease of acceptance is predicated [broadly], then no, because the predicate is perhaps more general. The truth is that fire is desiccating and not dry in the sense opposite to the sense found in water.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' description of the order of the four elements, followed by an objection and a detailed response. The author first relates the philosophers' claim: earth is surrounded by water, water by air, air by fire, and these are concentric spheres except for water. Motion is heating, so the body closest to the sphere (fire) is hottest and most subtle, and the farthest (earth) is coldest and densest. Air is second in subtlety, and water second in density. The author then objects: this arrangement implies that earth is colder than air, contradicting the philosophers' own view, and that fire should be extremely moist, whereas they define moisture as ease of accepting shapes, but air is not moist. Then the author inserts a marginal note (أقول) defending the philosophers: they do not attribute fire's heat to motion but to its form; the citation from al-Kabiri is unreliable, and Ibn Sina noted his instability. Regarding the objection about coldness, the philosophers do not explain coldness by distance but by density; earth is colder in itself but not sensed due to its density. As for moisture, if defined as ease of accepting shapes, then fire is not moist, but if moisture is broader, the objection fails. The note concludes that fire is desiccating, not dry in the sense opposite to water's moisture.

Correction: The response clarifies that they attribute it to fire's essential form, and the transmission from al-Kabiri is unreliable.

Checkpoint

The Issue of God's Essence and Two Opposing Views
Razi states the issue and reports two opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing views of Abu Hashim and Ibn Sina on whether God's essence is different from all other essences.

Core Idea
Two theologians claimed God's essence is equal to others, differing only by attributes or by a negative condition.

Before offering his own argument, Razi first presents the landscape of opinion. Two major scholars held that God's essence is not intrinsically different from other essences: Abu Hashim claimed equality in essentiality with a special state, while Ibn Sina said God's quiddity is existence itself, distinguished only by a negation. This lesson introduces those views so we can see what Razi is refuting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the issue
Notice the heading: this is part of the discourse on negative attributes. The issue is whether God's quiddity differs from all others.
2
Understand Abu Hashim's view
Abu Hashim said essences are equal; difference comes from a state that necessitates four attributes. Identify those attributes.
The attributes are life, knowledge, existence, and power.
3
Understand Ibn Sina's view
Ibn Sina claimed God's quiddity is existence itself, and existence is a common term. God is distinguished by a negative condition: His existence does not attach to a quiddity.
4
Compare the two views
Both views deny intrinsic difference. Abu Hashim uses a positive state; Ibn Sina uses a negative condition. Notice how they differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في السلوب . القسم الثاني في الصفات . مسألة : ماهية الله تعالى مخالفة لسائر الماهيات أم لا خلافا لأبي هاشم . فإنه قال ذاته مساوية لسائر الذوات في الذاتية وإنما تخالفها بحالة توجب أحوال أربعة هي الحية والعالمية والموجودية والقادرية ، خلافا لأبى على بن سينا إنه زعم أن ماهيته نفس الوجود والوجود مسمى مشترك بين كل الموجودات، وزعم أنه إنما امتاز عن الممكنات بقيد سلبى وهو أن وجوده غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات وسائر الوجودات عارضة : لنا أن مخالفته لغيره لو كانت بصفة لزمت المساواة بالذات ولو كان كذلك لكان اختصاص ذاته بما به يخالف غيرها إن لم يكن الأمر كان الجائز غنيا عن السبب وهو محال ، أو الأمر فيلزم التسلسل
The discourse on negative attributes. The second section on attributes. Issue: The quiddity (māhiyya) of God, exalted, is different from all other quiddities or not? Contrary to Abu Hashim. For he said that His essence is equal to all essences in essentiality, and only differs from them by a state (ḥāla) that necessitates four states: life (ḥayāt), knowledge (ʿālamiyya), existence (mawjūdiyya), and power (qādriyya). Contrary to Abu Ali ibn Sina, for he claimed that His quiddity is existence itself, and existence is a common name among all existents, and he claimed that He is only distinguished from possible beings by a negative condition: that His existence does not attach to any quiddity, while other existences attach [to quiddities]. For us: that His difference from other [quiddities] if it were by an attribute, then equality in essence would be necessitated, and if that were so, then the specification of His essence by that by which it differs from other [essences]—if it is not a matter [of necessity?], then the possible would be independent of a cause, which is impossible, or it is a matter, and then an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof regarding God's essence. It begins with a heading: 'The discourse on negative attributes' (القول في السلوب), which is the second section on attributes. Then the issue: is God's quiddity different from all other quiddities or not? Razi lists two opposing views: Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i held that God's essence is equal to other essences in essentiality, and only differs by a state (حالة) that necessitates four states: life, knowledge, existence, and power. Ibn Sina held that God's quiddity is existence itself, and existence is a common term, but God's existence is distinguished by a negative condition: it does not attach to any quiddity, while other existences do. Razi then gives his own proof: if God's difference from others were by an attribute, then there would be equality in essence. Then the specification of His essence by that attribute would either be without a cause (making the possible independent, which is impossible) or by another attribute, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل). Therefore, God's essence must be different in itself.

Key Terms
ماهية
ذات
حالة
وجود
عارض
سلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Life, will, power, speech
B) Life, knowledge, existence, power
C) Knowledge, power, justice, truth
D) Existence, knowledge, will, sight

Key Takeaway
Both Abu Hashim and Ibn Sina denied that God's essence is intrinsically different from other essences, offering alternative explanations based on states or negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you reflect on your own worship, do you tend to focus on God's attributes (like power, knowledge) rather than His unknowable essence? How does the debate between essence vs. attributes challenge you to deepen your reverence and humility before a God whose very quiddity is beyond comparison?

Razi's Proof Against Divine Essence Being Like Others
Razi gives a proof that God's essence is intrinsically different from others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that if God's essence were like others, it would lead to an infinite regress of attributes or an uncaused possibility.

Core Idea
Difference by attribute implies equality in essence, leading to infinite regress or an uncaused effect.

Having presented the opposing views, Razi now gives his own reasoning to prove that God's essence must be different in itself, not by an attribute or condition. He uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the difference were by an attribute, then that attribute would require another attribute, and so on, resulting in an infinite chain. This lesson unpacks that compact argument.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's proof carefully
The proof is a conditional argument. Assume the difference is by an attribute. Then what follows?
2
Understand the first consequence
If difference is by an attribute, then equality in essence is necessitated. That means God's essence is not different in itself.
3
Identify the two options for specification
Given equality in essence, the attribute that differentiates must either be without a cause (possible becoming independent, absurd) or caused by another attribute, leading to an infinite chain (tasalsul).
4
Recognize the conclusion
Since infinite regress is impossible and an uncaused possible is impossible, the initial assumption is false. Therefore God's essence is different in itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For he said that His essence is equal to all essences in essentiality, and only differs from them by a state (ḥāla) that necessitates four states: life (ḥayāt), knowledge (ʿālamiyya), existence (mawjūdiyya), and power (qādriyya). Then the issue: is God's quiddity different from all other quiddities or not? Misconception: One might think that if God's essence is different by an attribute, then the attribute itself does not need a cause.
Correction: Razi argues that the attribute itself must be specified, leading to either a violation of causality or an infinite regress.

?

The Distinctness of Allah's Essence
Imam Razi argues that Allah's essence is distinct, refuting Abu Hashim and Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that Allah's essence is fundamentally different from all other essences, and how he refutes the views of Abu Hashim and Ibn Sina.

Core Idea
Allah's essence is distinct in itself, not by added attributes.

Imam Razi opens with a crucial theological question: Is Allah's essence different from other essences? He presents two opposing views: Abu Hashim claims equality in essence with difference by states, and Ibn Sina identifies Allah's essence with existence itself. Then Razi offers a logical proof that any difference via an added attribute leads to absurdity, establishing that the difference must be intrinsic to the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the full Arabic passage once. Notice the question at the beginning and the refutations of two scholars.
The passage is a question followed by two opposing views and then a proof.
2
Identify the three positions
Identify Imam Razi's own position (essence is different), Abu Hashim's (equal in essence, difference by states), and Ibn Sina's (essence is existence).
Key terms: 'الماهية', 'الذات', 'أحوال'.
3
Analyze the proof
Work through the proof: if difference were by an attribute, then essences would be equal; then the specification of the essence with that attribute would need a cause; if no cause, impossible (uncaused contingent); if cause, infinite regress. Therefore, difference must be intrinsic.
This proof shows that difference cannot be by an added attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : ماهية الله تعالى مخالفة لسائر الماهيات أم لا؟ خلافا لابی هاشم فإنه قال ذاته مساوية لسائر الذوات في الذاتية وإنما تخالفها بحالة توجب أحوال أربعة هى الحية والعالمية والموجودية والقادرية ، خلافا لأبى على بن سينا إنه زعم أن ماهيته نفس الوجود والوجود مسمى مشترك بين كل الموجودات، وزعم أنه إنما امتاز عن الممكنات بقيد سلي وهو أن وجوده غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات وسائر الوجودات عارضة : لنا أن مخالفته لغيره لو كانت بصفة الحاصلة المساواة بالذات ولو كان كذلك لكان اختصاص ذاته بما به يخالف غيرها إن لم يكن الأمر كان الجائز غنيا عن السبب وهو محال ، أو الأمر فيلزم التسلسل.
Question: Is the essence of Allah different from other essences or not? Contrary to Abu Hashim, for he said His essence is equal to other essences in essence-hood and only differs from them by a state that necessitates four states: life, knowledge, existence, and power. Contrary to Abu Ali ibn Sina, for he claimed that His essence is existence itself, and existence is a common term among all existents, and he claimed that He is distinguished from possibles by a condition [؟] that His existence is not an accident to any essence while other existences are accidents. Our proof: If His difference from others were by an attribute that occurs, then equality in essence would follow. And if so, then the specification of His essence with that by which it differs from others—if that specification were not caused, then the possible would be independent of a cause—impossible; or if caused, then a regress follows.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a question and proof. Imam Razi states that Allah's essence is different from all other essences, contra Abū Hāshim who claimed equality in essence with difference by states (life, knowledge, existence, power), and contra Ibn Sīnā who claimed His essence is existence itself, which is common, distinguished only by being uncaused in essence. The proof: If the difference were by an attribute occurring to the essence, then essences would be equal in essence. Then the specification of this essence with that attribute would need a cause. If it had no cause, the possible essence would be independent—impossible. If it had a cause, then the attribute itself would require another specification, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, the difference must be in the essence itself, not by an additional attribute.

Key Terms
الماهية
الذات
أحوال
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah's essence is the same as other essences; the difference is only in attributes.
Correction: Imam Razi proves that if the difference were by an attribute, it would lead to an infinite regress or an uncaused contingent. Therefore, the difference must be in the essence itself, not merely in attributes.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi reject Abu Hashim's view?
A) Because attributes are not real.
B) Because it leads to an infinite regress or an uncaused contingent.
C) Because essences cannot be equal.
D) Because Abu Hashim denied divine attributes.

Key Takeaway
Allah's essence is fundamentally distinct from all other essences, not by added attributes but by its very nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever thought that Allah is 'like' other things but 'better,' how does Razi's argument that any added attribute leads to an impossible regress challenge that hidden assumption? Will you today consciously affirm that His essence is absolutely unique, beyond any comparison?
?
This proof shows that Allah's distinctness is not by a characteristic He happens to have, but by His very essence. When you call upon Him as 'the Distinct' (al-Wāḥid), do you truly feel the weight of that uniqueness? What ordinary comparison do you need to let go of to honor this truth?
?
Razi's argument leaves no room for mental pictures that reduce Allah to a concept. Is there an image or analogy you have unknowingly placed in your heart that equates His essence to something created? Take a moment to repent from that, and ask Allah to increase your conviction in His incomparability.

Logical Refutation of Temporal Accidents in God's Essence
Razi's initial logical refutation of the Karramiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction in claiming that temporal accidents could be eternally possible in God's essence.

Core Idea
Eternal possibility of temporal accidents is a contradiction.

Imam Razi tackles a subtle theological dispute with the Karramiyya, who held that temporal accidents could occur in God. He begins by exposing a fundamental logical flaw: if the possibility of God being described by a temporal accident were inherent in His essence, it would require eternal possibility of a temporal thing, which is impossible. This lesson walks through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sect being refuted
The passage starts by stating the position in dispute: the Karramiyya claim that temporal accidents can subsist in God's essence. Note that this is a theological innovation.
The Karramiyya were an anthropomorphist sect.
2
Understand the conditional premise
The author presents a conditional: if the possibility of being described by a temporal accident were a necessity of God's quiddity (essence), then that possibility would be eternal.
Quiddity refers to the reality of something, its 'whatness'.
3
See the impossibility
An eternal possibility of a temporal thing is impossible because eternity (al-azal) is a negation of beginning, while temporality (al-huduth) is its affirmation. Combining them leads to contradiction.
This is the core logical flaw.
4
Contrast with the opponent's view
If the Karramiyya insist that God can be described by temporal accidents, they must either accept eternal possibility (which is impossible) or deny that the possibility is from the quiddity. The author forces this dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : لا يجوز قيام الحوادث بذات الله تعالى خلافا للكرامية الأولية اتصافه بها لكانت تلك الصحة من لوازم ماهيته فيلم حصول تلك الصحة أزلا امكن ذلك محال اذا لوصح الأن صحة اتصافه بها أزلا يتوقف على صحة وجودها أزلا وذلك محال ، لأن الأزل عبارة عن نفى والحدوث عبارة عن ثبوتها والجمع بينهما محال فإن قيل هذا يشكل بما أن العالم جائز الوجود لذاته ولم يلزم جواز وجوده لذلك أزلا فكذا هنا ثم . نقول صحة اتصاف الذات بالصفة عن صحة وجود وجود الصفة فى نفسها ، ولا يلزم من ثبوت أحد يهما ثبوت الأخرى . فإنا نقول : يصبح اتصاف الذات أزلا بهذه الصفة لو كانت في نفسها ممكنة كانت الذات قابلة لهذا وهذا لا يستدعى كون الصفة في نفسها صحيحة ، ثم نقول ماذكرته إن دل على قولك فهنا ما يدل على قولنا من وجوه الأول هو أن العالم محدث فالله لم يكن فاعلا للعالم أزلا الان الفاعل ولا فعل محال ثم صار فاعلا والفاعلية صفة ثبوتية فهذا يقتضى حدوث هذه الصفة في ذات الله تعالى
Question: It is not permissible for temporal accidents to subsist in God's essence, contrary to the Karramiyya. [Argument:] If the possibility of His being described by it were from the necessities of His quiddity, the attainment of that possibility eternally would be possible—that is impossible, because if the possibility of His being described by it now is valid, its validity eternally depends on the validity of its existence eternally, and that is impossible, because eternity is a negation, and temporality is its affirmation, and combining them is impossible. If it is said: This is problematic because the world is possible in itself, and its possibility of existence does not necessitate its existence eternally; similarly here. Then we say: The validity of the essence being described by an attribute depends on the validity of the existence of the attribute in itself, and the establishment of one does not necessitate the establishment of the other. For we say: The essence can be described eternally by this attribute even if it is possible in itself; the essence is receptive to this, and this does not require the attribute to be valid in itself. Then we say: What you mentioned, if it indicates your position, then here is what indicates our position from aspects: First is that the world is originated, so God was not an agent of the world eternally—since an agent without act is impossible—then He became an agent, and agency is a positive attribute; this necessitates the origination of this attribute in God's essence.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Karramiyya claim that temporal accidents (hawadith) can subsist in God's essence. The argument begins with a conditional: if the possibility of God being described by a temporal accident were from the necessities of His essence, then that possibility would be eternal, but that is impossible because the existence of the accident itself is temporal, and eternal possibility of a temporal thing leads to a contradiction between eternity (negation) and temporality (affirmation). An objection is raised: the world is possible in itself yet it did not exist eternally; similarly, the possibility of the attribute does not require its eternal existence. The response distinguishes between the possibility of the essence being described (which depends on the attribute's possibility) and the actual existence of the attribute. The essence can be eternally receptive to an accident without the accident being eternal. Then the author gives a positive proof: the world is temporal, so God's attribute of being an agent is temporal; agency is a positive attribute, so it must have originated in God's essence. Thus, temporal accidents do occur in God's essence according to the opponent's own admission, which they try to avoid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can change or acquire new attributes over time, like the Karramiyya.
Correction: The argument shows that if God could be described by temporal accidents, the possibility of that must be eternal, which is contradictory. Therefore, God's essence cannot be a locus for temporal accidents.

Checkpoint
What is the 'impossible combination' mentioned in the passage?
A) Eternity and temporality
B) Possibility and impossibility
C) Essence and attributes
D) Negation and affirmation

Key Takeaway
Eternal possibility of temporal accidents is a logical impossibility, so the Karramiyya claim fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you ever doubt because you cannot see change in God's response to your prayers? Confess honestly: do you want a God who changes according to your mood, or the unchanging God of this passage?
?
If God's knowledge is not subject to temporality, then He knows your entire life from before time. How does that make you feel about your own choices? Are you willing to accept that His decree is fixed, even when you want to change your fate? Examine your heart: is there an area where you resist this truth because you want control?
?
The Karramiyya thought God could be described by temporal events. Razi shows that would contradict His eternity. Think about your own understanding: do you ever imagine God as 'reacting' to your actions as if He were in time? How does this passage challenge that? Are you ready to worship a God whose essence is beyond time, even if it feels less personal? What does that demand of your faith?

Response to Objection and Positive Proof from Agency
Razi's response and positive proof from agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the objection regarding the world's possibility and the positive proof that God's attribute of agency is temporal.

Core Idea
God's agency as a temporal attribute proves temporal accidents in His essence.

Having presented the logical refutation, Imam Razi anticipates an objection: the world is possible in itself but not eternal; similarly, the possibility of a temporal attribute does not require its eternal existence. He responds by distinguishing between the possibility of description and the existence of the attribute. Then he offers a positive proof: the world is originated, so God's attribute of being an agent must have originated in His essence, implying temporal accidents do occur in God according to the opponent's own logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent argues that just because the world is possible in itself does not mean it must exist eternally; similarly, the possibility of a temporal attribute in God does not require its eternal existence.
This objection challenges the earlier argument.
2
Analyze Razi's response
Razi distinguishes between the validity of the essence being described (which depends on the attribute's possibility) and the actual existence of the attribute. The essence can be eternally receptive to an accident without the accident itself being eternal.
3
Identify the positive proof
Razi gives a positive proof from agency: the world is temporal, so God was not an agent eternally (since agent without act is impossible). Therefore, God's attribute of agency (al-fa'iliyya) must have originated in His essence, proving that temporal accidents do subsist in God.
This proof is actually from the opponent's own premise about the world's temporality.
4
Consider the implications
If the Karramiyya accept that God's agency is a temporal attribute, then they must admit that temporal accidents inhere in God's essence, which they deny. This forces them into inconsistency.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Impossibility of Accidents Subsisting in God
The author argues against the Karramiyya that accidents cannot exist in God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidents cannot subsist in God's essence according to the argument presented against the Karramiyya.

Core Idea
Accidents are temporal; God is eternal; they cannot coexist.

This passage addresses a key theological dispute with the Karramiyya sect, who argued that accidents could subsist in God. The author presents a logical argument based on the impossibility of combining eternal and temporal attributes. We'll examine the argument, an objection, and the rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: position, argument, objection, response.
The OCR is corrupt, but try to follow the logical flow.
2
Understand the main argument
Identify the core premise: eternity is negation, temporality is affirmation, and combining them is impossible. How does this refute the Karramiyya?
3
Follow the objection and response
The objection uses the analogy of the world's possibility. The response distinguishes between the possibility of description and the possibility of attribute existence. Then note the additional proof from agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(مسألة : لا يجوز قيام الحوادث بذات الله تعالى خلافا للكرامية) الأولية [؟] اتصافه بها لكانت تلك الصحة من لوازم ماهيته فيلم [؟] حصول تلك الصحة أزلا امكن ذلك محال اذا لوصح الأن صحة اتصافه بها أزلا يتوقف على صحة وجودها أزلا وذلك محال ، لأن الأزل عبارة عن نفى والحدوث عبارة عن ثبوتها والجمع بينهما محال . فإن قيل هذا يشكل بما أن العالم جائز الوجود لذاته ولم يلزم جواز وجوده لذلك أزلا فكذا هنا . ثم نقول صحة اتصاف الذات بالصفة عن [؟] صحة وجود الصفة فى نفسها ، ولا يلزم من ثبوت أحد يهما [؟] ثبوت الأخرى . فإنا نقول : يصبح اتصاف الذات أزلا بهذه الصفة لو كانت في نفسها ممكنة كانت الذات قابلة لهذا وهذا لا يستدعى كون الصفة في نفسها صحيحة ، ثم نقول ماذكرته إن دل على قولك فهنا ما يدل على قولنا من وجوه الأول هو أن العالم محدث فالله لم يكن فاعلا للعالم أزلا الان [؟] الفاعل ولا فعل محال ثم صار فاعلا والفاعلية صفة ثبوتية فهذا يقتضى حدوث هذه الصفة في ذات الله تعالى
(Question: It is not permissible for accidents to subsist in the essence of God Almighty, contrary to the Karramiyya.) Firstly, had He been described by them, that possibility would be a necessary concomitant of His essence, so obtaining that possibility from eternity would be possible, and that is impossible, because if now the possibility of His being described by them from eternity depends on the possibility of their existence from eternity, and that is impossible, because eternity is an expression of negation and temporality is an expression of their affirmation, and combining them is impossible. If it is said: This is problematic because the world is possible in itself, and its possibility of existence from eternity does not follow, so here too. Then we say: The possibility of the essence being described by the attribute is [something else] besides the possibility of the existence of the attribute in itself, and the affirmation of one does not entail the affirmation of the other. For we say: The essence being described from eternity by this attribute would be possible if it were possible in itself, the essence being receptive to this, and this does not require the attribute to be possible in itself. Then we say: What you have mentioned, if it indicates your view, then here is what indicates our view in some ways. The first is that the world is temporally originated, so God was not an agent of the world from eternity, because an agent without an act is impossible, then He became an agent, and agency is a positive attribute, so this necessitates the origination of this attribute in the essence of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the position that accidents (hawadith) cannot subsist in God's essence, contrary to the Karramiyya. The author presents a logical argument: if it were possible for God to be described by accidents, that possibility would be a necessary concomitant of His essence, and thus it would be possible from eternity. But that is impossible because eternity negates temporal origination. An objection is raised: just as the world is possible in itself but not necessarily existent from eternity, so here the possibility of description does not require actuality from eternity. The author responds by distinguishing between the possibility of the essence being described and the possibility of the attribute existing in itself; one does not entail the other. Then he provides a proof from the author's own school: the world is originated, so God was not an agent from eternity, then He became an agent, and agency is a positive attribute that is originated in God. This is the first of several arguments.

Ibn al-Haytham's Claim of Direction and Its Refutation
The author reports Ibn al-Haytham's directionalist view and critiques the argument from tahayyuz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Haytham's claim that God is in a direction above the Throne and the initial critique based on the problem of specification.

Core Idea
Direction for God leads to impossible consequences.

This lesson begins by presenting Ibn al-Haytham's view that Allah is in a direction above the Throne with infinite distance, as well as the variations among his followers. The author then critiques the argument based on occupying space (tahayyuz) as merely repeating the claim, and discusses the difficulty of specifying a place for God by choice without an additional factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Pay attention to the different positions attributed to Ibn al-Haytham and his followers.
The OCR is corrupt; rely on the translation.
2
Identify the main claim
Ibn al-Haytham claims God is in a direction above the Throne with infinite distance. Note the variations: some say finite distance, some say He is upon the Throne.
3
Understand the critique of the tahayyuz argument
The author says that basing the negation of direction on the concept of occupying space is just repeating the claim, not proving it.
Tahayyuz means occupying space.
4
Analyze the specification problem
If God chooses a place, this does not require an additional factor to specify it, similar to the problem of choosing between two equals.
This is a Kalām argument about divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المصنف [؟] · ه ابن الهيضم إنه تعالى في جهة فوق الارش [؟] لا نهاية لها والبعد بينه وبين العرض أيضا غير متناه ، وقال أصحابه البعد متناه وكلهم نفوا عنه خمسا من الجهات وأثبتوا له التحت الذى هو مكان غيره ، وباقى أصحاب ابن الهيضم قالوا بكونه على العرش كما قال سائر المجسمة وبعضهم قالوا بكونه على صورة وقالوا بمجيئه وذهابه واستدلال بنى التحيز على نفى الجهة إعادة الدعوى والاختصاص بمكان هو فيه إن كان باختياره لا يقتضى أمرا زائدا تخصيصه به كما قالوا فى اختياره أحد المتساويين من غير ترجح والمكان إن لم يكن وجوديا كان كونه في المكان أزلا غير منكر على تقدير إمكان تحيره ، ومخالفة مكانه لسائر الأمكنة لا يقتضى كون مكانه موجودا ، فإن المدميات [؟] تتخالف بحسب تخالف ما ينسب إليه وإن كان المكان غير مشار إليه لم يحب [؟] من ذلك كون المتمكن غير مشار إليه فإنه من الجائز أن يصيرا هند [؟] التمكن مشارا إليهما، كما يقال فى الصورة والهيولى . والإشكال الذى أورد على المكان على تقدير كونه مشارا إليه بأنه إما أن يكون جسما أو عرضا ليس يختص بهذا الموضع بل هو وارد على أمكنة جميع الاجسام . وههنا قسم آخر وهو أن يكون خلاء لولا الجسم وقد مر فيه ما كان ينبغى أن يقال فيه والمعتمد منها أن الكائن في الجهة قابل للقسمة والأشكال وغير منفك من الأكوان ، وكل ذلك محال في حق واجب الوجود . (1) أقول الذى ذكر عام فى المواضع المتعارضة عقلا ونقلا وذلك كما ذكره.
The author [said] concerning Ibn al-Haytham that He Almighty is in a direction above the Throne, having no end, and the distance between Him and the Throne is also infinite. His companions said the distance is finite, and all of them negated five directions from Him and affirmed for Him the 'under' which is a place other than Him. The rest of Ibn al-Haytham's companions said He is upon the Throne as the rest of the corporealists said, and some said He is in a form, and they spoke of His coming and going. The argument of those who base on [the concept of] occupying space (tahayyuz) to negate direction is a repetition of the claim. The specification of a place in which He is, if it is by His choice, does not necessitate an additional matter specifying Him with it, as they said concerning His choice of one of two equals without preference. And if the place is not existent, then His being in a place from eternity is not objectionable on the assumption of the possibility of His inhabiting it. The difference of His place from other places does not necessitate that His place is existent, for non-existent things differ according to the difference of that to which they are attributed. And if the place is not an object of reference, it does not follow from that that the one located is not an object of reference, for it is possible that both the location and the located become objects of reference, as is said concerning form and matter. The problem that was raised concerning place on the assumption that it is an object of reference—that it is either a body or an accident—is not specific to this topic, but applies to the places of all bodies. Here is another section: that it is a void if not for the body, and the discussion has passed concerning what should be said about it. The relied-upon [argument] among them is that whatever is in a direction is divisible and subject to shapes and is not free from accidents (akwan), and all of that is impossible with respect to the Necessary Existent. (1) I say: what has been mentioned is general in topics that are opposed rationally and traditionally, and that is as he mentioned.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Ibn al-Haytham's views on God being in a direction. The author first reports Ibn al-Haytham's claim that God is in a direction above the Throne with infinite distance, and the variations among his followers. Then he examines various arguments. He critiques the argument based on tahayyuz (occupying space) as merely repeating the claim. He discusses the possibility that God's choice of a place does not require an additional specifying factor, similar to the problem of choosing between equals. He considers the possibility of place being non-existent and argues that being in a non-existent place from eternity is not necessarily objectionable. He then addresses the issue of reference: even if place is not an object of reference, the located entity might still be an object of reference, using the analogy of form and matter. Finally, he presents the decisive argument: anything in a direction is divisible, subject to shapes, and inseparable from accidents (akwan), all of which are impossible for the Necessary Existent. The gloss notes that this is a general principle for dealing with conflicting rational and traditional evidence.

C) It is based on scripture.
D) It establishes direction.

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Haytham's directionalist view lacks a rigorous rational foundation.

If God’s choice of a place does not require an additional cause, then what does this say about His absolute freedom? How does this truth demand a change in how you understand God's actions in your own life—especially events you find difficult or arbitrary?
